FOR OMCIAL USE ONLY
NDIA
UPPER GANGA IRRIGATION MODERNIZATION PROJECT
Credit and Project Summary
Borrower:              India, acting by its President (GOI).
Beneficiary:           Government of Uttar Pradesh (GOUP)
Amount:                IDA Credit:  SDR 117.5 M
(US$125.0 M equivalent)
Terms:                 IDA Credit:  Standard
Re-LendinR Terms:      From GOI to GOUP as part of central assistance to
States of India for development projects on terms
and conditions applicable at the time. GOI would
bear the foreign exchange risk.
Project Description: The proposed project comprises a six-year tilme-slice
(September 1984 - September 1990) of the rehabili-
tation and modernization the Upper Ganga Canal (UGC)
System. It would (a) replace the existing main canal
with a parallel lined canal together with modern
regulating and access structures primarily to avert
risk to the existing irrigated areas; (b) modernize
the distribution system serving about 43,000 ha with
lined channels down to and including watercourses; (c)
construct augmentation ttibevells on the lined
distributaries to supplement surface water supplies;
(d) undertake a reconnaissance drainage study for the
UGC command area and a 1etailed drainage study and
pilot works program within the distributary blocks to
be modernized; (e) initiate construction of a modern
canal communication system; (f) construct one small
hydro-electric station on the main canal and initiate
the civil works of another station; (g) set up a water
and land management. institute; (h) strengthen the
project organization for planning, design, monitoring
and evaluation and management of UGC modernization
program; train project staff, provide study tours,
visits and technical assistance; and Ci) provide
vehicles, plant and equipment for the construction
and managment of the project.
The project would alleviate risks to the existing
irrigated area and increase agricultural production
and the standard of living of farmers in UP. Possible
risks under the proposed project are these normally
associated with irrigation project in India.

INDIA
UPPER GANGA IRRIGATION MODERNIZATION PROJECT
Staff Appraisal Report
I. BACKGROUND
Introduction
1.01    The State of Uttar Pradesh (UP), located in the northern central part
of India, is well endowed with water resources (Map IBRD 17646R). However,
only about two-fifths of the cropped area is irrigated in spite of large
public investments in surface water and tubewell irrigation as well as rapid
private groundwater development. Consequently, the Government of Uttar
Pradesh (GOUP) has placed great emphasis on the acceleration of irrigation in
recent Five-Year Plans. In western UP, available surface water resources
have been fully committed. Groundwater development has been carried out
aggressively by both the private and public sectors and this resource is now
fairly highly exploited.  Further expansion of agricultural production tbere-
fore will depend mainly on improved efficiency and performance of existing
irrigation systems. The proposed Upper Ganga Irrigation Modernization
Project would focus on this requirement.
Agriculture and IrriRation in India
1.02    India has a population of about 700 M (mid-1982) which is growing
at an annual rate of about 2.0. Since 1960, per capita income grew at an
annual rate of 1.4Z and reached US$240 in 1979/80. Although the average per
capita income has increased and access to public services has improved,
growth has been too slow to bring about more than a gradual reduction in the
incidence of poverty. Accordingly, GOI's development plans give priority to
alleviating poverty and creating employment, especially in rural areas.
1.03    Agriculture is the dominant sector of the Indian economy and con-
tributes about 40% of GNP.  It engages about 70Z of the labor force and
provides the base for about 55% of India's exports. During the last decade,
GOI has devoted considerable attention to agriculture in its development
plans. To support agricultural growth, GOI has: (i) accelerated irrigation
development through modernization of existing schemes and construction of new
schemes; and (ii) improved agricultural supporting services to optimize the
use of land and water resources.
1.04    Irrigation was given the highest priority from the beginning of the
planning era in 1950.  Some 52.6 M ha of irrigated command were developed
by 1979/80, about 58% of which is from surface water sources and 422 from
groundwater. Irrigated areas are almost four times as productive as rainfed
areas and they account for about 60% of all agricultural output in India.
More significantly, the expansion of irrigation facilities and the increased
productivity on irrigated lands have together accounted for at least
three-quarters of the agricultural growth since 1960. The potential area
developed for irrigation is expected to reach 66.2 M ha by the end of
1985/86, of which 56% will be from surface water sources and 44% from
groundwater. With little idle land left, only limited increase is expected
I                                               't                 I



-2-
in the net sown area in the future. The main increase in agricultural
production will have to come from full utilization of potential area
developed for irrigation (from double cropping, improvemenLs in agricultural
practices, water management and the use of fertilizers and pesticides). In
most completed projects, actual irrigated areas are less than planned and
yields have been much lower than originally forecast. GOI and the State
Governments are aware of the challenge and are seeking solutions to the dual
problem of low irrigation intensities and poor crop yields.
1.05    Irrigation projects of the 1950s and 60s typically involved a storage
or diversion dam and only the main conveyance system, leaving the farmers
to construct the distribution system from project outlets to individual
fields. In most projects, farmers were unable to finance or organize con-
struction of the minor channels without outside assistance and this was
rarely available. This resulted in considerable delays in the utilization of
water from the irrigation potential created.
1.06    In the early 1970s, the concept of the Command Area Development
Authority (CADA) evolved. Its objectives were to coordinate the State
irrigation and agriculture activities and to assist farmers in obtaining
institutional credit for the construction of the minor irrigation systems and
for land development. The results of this approach have been below expeota-
tions. Coordination was not entirely effective, and the attempt to finance
on-farm development through institutional credit was generally unsuccessful.
Farmers had no confidence in the reliability of water supply and were there-
fore reluctant to undertake loan obligations, since no facility or framework
for effective group action had been developed. This experience indicates
that (i) more emphasis is needed on improving the efficiency and reliability
of the conveyance system from the water source to the irrigation outlets;
and (ii) Government should assume responsibility for conveying water much
closer to the farmgate. There are clear indications that when individual
farmers are fully confident of their water supply, increased cooperation
results and they will undertake investments in other agricultural inputs and
make full use of the water supplied. These concepts have been used in the
design of the proposed project.
The State of Uttar Pradesh
1.07    Salient Features.  The State of Uttar Pradesh (UP) has a population
of about 111 M (1981). This makes it the most populous State in India
accounting 5or 16% of total population, whereas its geographical area
(298,000 km ) covers 9% of the country. The population density is extremely
high at 377 persons per km2.  About two thirds of all farm holdings in UP are
below 1.0 ha as compared to about 50% for the nation.
1.08    By most traditional indicators, UP is one of the least advanced
States in India. The literacy rate (27.4%; 1981) is lower than the national
average of 36.0%. The number of doctors and health centers per 1,000 persons
is among the lowest in India. Although the State is well connected to the
rest of India through national highways, railways and airways, the
intra-State communication system is inadequate. The provision of potable
water has been slow. At present, water supply schemes are being implemented



-3-
in about 202 of the villages which lack an adequate source of drinking water.
The Bank Group is supporting GOUP in water supply schemes through the
on-going Uttar Pradesh Water Supply and Sewerage Project (Cr. 585-IN;
US$40 M).
1.09    The Economy.  The economy of UP is predominantly agrarian with
agriculture contributing about 56% of the State income and employing about
782 of the work force. Between 1960/61 and 1976/77, the rate of growth of
the primary sector, including agriculture, forestry and mining was only 1.7Z
per annum (p.a.) compared to an all-India average of 2.02. Similarly, the
secondary sector, including manufacturing, construction, electricity, gas and
water supply (152 of the State income), only grew at 22 p.a., which was half
the national rate of 4%. The performance of the tertiary sector, including
transport, trade, finance and services (30Z of the State income), fared
bette-r at 42 growth p.a. However, since tertiary sector activities started
from a relatively small base and were concentrated mostly in the urban areas,
their rapid growth contributed only marginally to majority of population
(822) living in the rural areas. As a result of poor performance of the
primary and secondary sectors, State income grew at an annual rate of 2.2Z
p.a., well below the national rate of 3.12.   Per capita income only
increased by 0.52 p.a. and was one of the lowest in India (Rs. 981 in
1979/80), compared to the national average of Rs. 1,267. Since agriculture
is the main activity in the State and it has close links to other sectors,
economic progress is largely dependent on the removal of constraints to
further agricultural growth, particularly through the expansion of irriga-
tion.
1.10    Employment and Rural Poverty.  The labor force constitutes about 312
of the population of UP. Its participation rate is significantly higher in
rural areas (31.42) than in urban areas (27.72). Cultivators and agricul-
tural laborers account for 652 and 22.82 of the rural labor force, respec-
tively. Because of the slow growth of agriculture and manufacturing, produc-
tive employment has failed to keep pace with population growth. The poor
performance of the agricultural sector also resulted in a sharp rise in the
proportion of rural population living below the poverty line (currently about
US$90 per capita per annum) from 41.62 in 1960161 to 63.62 in 1970/71.
According to the National Sample Survey in 1973/74, about two-thirds of the
rural population in UP lived below the poverty level and an estimated 90Z of
agricultural laborers are witbin this group. A series of programs have been
created to raise standards of living of rural poor, including measures to
secure tenants' rights, land redistribution and public works programs. These
programs have had limited success due to the intrinsic difficulties of the
problem, faulty planning and poor implementation.
1.11    ARricultural Performance (Table T-1).  From the comencement of the
First Five-Year Plan (1950/51 to 1977/78), foodgrain production grew only at
about 22 p.a., barely keeping pace with population growth. Rice and wheat
production accounted for most of this growth while coarse grains stagnated
and pulse production declined. The performance of non-foodgrain crops such
as oilseeds, sugarcane and potato fared better at 3.1Z, 3.5% and 5.5% growth
p.a., respectively. However, since these are minor crops, their rapid rates



-4-
of growth do not significantly affect the overall performance of the agricul-
tural sector.
1.12    The early years after Independence were characterized by both area
expansion and yield increases for most crops. Between 1950/51 and 1960/61,
rice and wheat production (major cereals in the State) grew at respectable
rates of 5.2Z and 4.8X pa. respectively. In the 1960s, the agricultural
scene changed significantly with increases in production of most crops due
largely to riLing yields; wheat and maize production grew at 9.92 and 9.72
p.a, respectively. Oilseeds and sugarcane production only grew at 3.72 and
1.02 p.a., respectively. Gain in potato production was spectacular at 102
p.a., resulting from both area expansion (4.32 p.a.) and yield increases
(5.62 p.a.). The impressive performance of most crops, except rice and
sugarcane, during this period was largely due to the introduction of hig8
yielding varieties and accelerated development of private tubewells in the
latter part of the 1960s.
1.13    The transformation in rice production seemed to lag a few years
behind wheat. From 1971 to 1978, rice production improved significantly,
growing at 4.72 p.a., slightly above wheat production (4.12). Production of
areas under otber cereal crops such as maize and barley declined. Cash crops
performed well during this period, groving at 4-5X p.a. Production of oil-
seeds and sugarcane improved significantly, with growths of 4.22 and 4.42
p.a., respectively. Potato production maintained its earlier momentum,
growing at 9.52 p.s.
1.14    Irritation Development. The State is well endowed with water resour-
ces. Nevertbeless, only about 8.9 N ha (522) of the total net cultivated
area of 17.0 M ha was irrigated in 1979180. Between 1950/51 and the onset of
the Green Revolution, surface and groundwater development contributed equally
to the expansion of irrigated areas. However, since 1965/66, groundwater
development has accounted for virtually all increases in net irrigated area.
In 1979/80, about 5.7 M ha (642) of the total net irrigated area was supplied
with groundwater compared to 2.8 N ha provided by large scale surface water
irrigation schemes. The remainder is served by storage tanks or by pump lift
schemes from rivers. Progress of irrigation development in terms of net
irrigated area in the State since 1950151 is shown below:

1.15    About 10.4 N ha or 402 of the gross cropped area (25.0 M ha) is
presently irrigated and wheat accounts for the largest share of the expansion
of irrigated area since Independence. The general trend for major crops has
been as follows:

1.16    Power SuDly and Demand  Uttar Pradesh is well endowed with natural
resources for generating power. However, due to limited financial resources,
the State has only installed 3,710 MW of generation of which about 30% is
provided by hydel schemes and the balance by thermal stations. Per capita
generation of electricity was about 115 kWh in 1979/80 compared to an
all-India average of 164 kWh. Per capita consumption of electricity rose
from about 7 kWh/annum at the commencement of the First Five-Year Plan (1951)
to 87 kWh/annum by 1579/80. This still lags behind the national average of
130 kWh/annum per capita. The progress of rural electrification has been
slow and presently only about one-third of the villages has electric power.
1.17    Agriculture accounts for about 34% of the total energy consumption
in the State. Public tubewells account for about 15% of the energy used in
the agricultural sector (i.e., 4% of total statewide energy use). The rapid
expansion of private tubewells over the last two decades has resulted in an
ever-increasing (and difficult to control) demand on the power transmission
capacity in rural areas. The consequence is that rural power availability
now averages about eight hours supply per day with large variations in both
running hours per day and quality of the supply depending on location within
the State.
II. THE PROJECT AREA AND PRESENT DEVELOPMENT
Salient Features
2.01    Location. The Upper Ganga Canal (UGC) system lies between the Ganga
and Yamuna Rivers in western UP and covers 10 of the 57 administrative dis-
tricts of the State. The southern boundary of the project is the alignment
of the Lower Ganga main canal (Map IBRD 17648R).
2.02    Climate.  The project area has a sub-tropical monsoon climate charac-
terized by wet, hot summers and relatively warm dry winters (Table T-2).
Average annual rainfall varies from about 1050 mm in the northern part of the
project area to about 650 mm in the southwest (Map IBRD 17647R). The south-
western monsoon, which contributes nearly 80% of the annual rainfall, nor-
mally starts in mid-June and generally withdraws by the end of September.



-6-
The monsoon shows considerable annual variations in amounts and distribution
of precipitation and the times of onset and termination are also uncertain.
Long dry spells may occur within the rainy season. Uncertainty regarding
amount and distribution of rainfall makes irrigation a prerequisite for
successful crop production even during the monsoon season. The winter season
of December to February has mean minimum daily temperatures in the range of
6-100C. This is followed by very hot and dry weather in May and June with
daily maximums exceeding 410C.
2.03    Topography and Soils.  Tne project area is gently undulating except
for the northern part where the streams from the Shivalik foothills are
deeply incised. Ground elevation falls from about 280 m above mean sea level
(m.s.l.) in the north to about 150 m above m.s.l. in the south. Natural
drainage is provided by numerous streams which flow roughly north to south
into the '-cundary rivers. Within the project area, there are low lands or
'khadars' formed where the rivers used to meander in the past. Most khadars
have saline soils but are generally underlain by fresh groundwater.
2.04    The soils of the project area are alluvial.  In Saharanpur and Meerut
districts, they are deep, dark grey, loam to loamy sands. In the southern
part (Mainpuri district), they are slightly acidic loams and sandy loans.
Saline and alkaline efflorsences, known as 'usar' and 'reh' occur in Aligarh,
Mainpuri and Etah districts (5-6% of the CCA) but are not major problems.
Fertility levels are medium to high.
2.05    Land Use.  The UGC system has a gross command area of about 1.95 M ha
of which the net commandable area is about 0.98 1 ha.  About 0.58 M ha is non
cultivable; of the remaining 0.39 M ha about 0.13 M ha is commanded by direct
irrigation public tubewells and 0.26 M ha will be served by the Madhya Ganga
Canal system presently under construction (para 2.10).
2.06    Population. Land Holdinus and Land Tenure.  Rural population density
is about 470 persons per km4. The lowest densities occur in Mathura district
(324) and the highest in Ghaziabad district (506). The average farm size in
the project area is about 0.8 ha which is considerably smaller than the State
average of 1.2 ha. Holdings of less than 2 ha account for about 77% of the
number of farms but only 36% of the total area. There are a variety of land
tenure arrangements. An Act in the-early 1950s abolished intermediate
tenancy and provided security of -.enure to former tenants. In 1961, a second
Act imposed a ceiling on rural landholdings with the specific aim of achiev-
ing greater equality in the landholdings of individual cultivators. However,
legal loopholes, coupled with enforcement difficulties, have meant that this
Act has been ineffective in achieving its objective. In 1971, the land
reform issue was reopened but so far has remained unresolved.
2.07    Roads.  The National Highway system crosses the project area from
Delhi towards Dehra Dun to the north, Lucknow to the east and southwards
towards Aligarh. There is also an extensive State highway network which is,
like the National Highways, reasonably well maintained. District Council and
village roads connect the smaller towns and villages to the State and
National road grid but many are unsurfaced and not motorable after prolonged



-7-
rain in the monsoon season. Unsurfaced roads are generally always usable by
animal transport, the principal means of moving farm produce.' The heaviest
burden placed on the road system is during sugarcane harvest but, since the
rainfall at that time is very low, there is no need for additional road
building or upgrading under the project.
Water Resources and Utilization
2.08    Water Resources.  The project area is wholly contained within the
drainage basin of the river Ganga system 1/ which is both an international
(Nepal, China, Bangladesh and India) and an inter-state (seven Indian States)
river. Evaluation of the hydrology and existing development on the Ganga
shows that the proposed project is not harmful to the interests of other
riparians in terms of disputes presently recorded (Annex 7, SDV). The UGC
takes off the river Ganga at Hardwar, where the upstream catchment is about
3% of the Ganga basin. The a nual average river flow at the project diver-
sion point is about 17,500 Mm (14.19 MAF); all low flow of the jiver
(October to May) is diverted into the UGC system (about 2,150 Mm ). In the
high flow period (June to September), diversion into the UGC is governed by
main canal capacity agd averages about 2,750 Nm3 with diversions in the zaid
season about 1,770 Mm .
2.09    Surface Irrigation.  The UGC was constructed during 1830-54 and was
the first large scale irrigation project in Uttar Pradesh. Originally it was
designed to divert dry season flows from the river Ganga to provide protec-
tive irrigation in the rabi season. The design of the system reflects the
technical requirements and capabilities as well as the social needs and
objectives of 19th century India. Piecemeal improvements have been made over
the years, but no overall modernization program has been undertaken to ensure
the most efficient-use of the available water supplies. The UGC system was
originally supplied from temporary diversions until 1920, when the first
diversion weir (Bhimgoda Barrage) was completed. Bhingoda Barrage is now
being replaced by a modern structure due to be completed in 1984/85. The
original diversion discharge of the UGC was 189 cumecs (6,750 cusecs) but
this has been progressively increased to about 294 cumecs. The system con-
sists of about 6,540 km of canals with a nominal design duty of about
1 cfs/100 ha. Under present conditions, the system can provide barely two
irrigations during the rabi season when river water supplies are low. In
kharif, canal water supplies generally exceed demand except at the start and
end of the season.
2.10    In addition to the UGC system, along the length of the river Ganga
that forms a boundary of the UGC command area, there are (existing, under
construction or planned) five other major canal systems with a combined
diversion capacity of about 1,204 cumecs. The geographical location of these
developments near the project area is shown schematically on Chart C-1.  The
j] The Ganga basin is one of the world's largest watersheds, covering about
one million Km2, of which about 85% is within India and forms about 26%
of India's territory.



-8-
existing Lower Ganga canal (238 cumecs) and the Parallel Lower Ganga canal
(126 cumecs) take off from the Ganga on the right bank at Narora Barrage and
form the southern boundary of the UGC project area (Map IBRD 17648R). The
Madhya Ganga (I) canal (232 cumecs) is under construction and cuts through
the project area to serve about 0.26 M ha which is presently unirrigated; in
addition, the MGC augments water supplies to some parts of the UGC command
area only in the kharif season when the river Ganga is in flood. On the left
bank, at Bhimgoda Barrage, the Eastern Ganga canal (134 cumecs) is under
construction. Also planned on the left bank of the Ganga is the Madhya Ganga
(II) canal (190 cumecs). Upstream of Bhimgoda Barrage, GOUP has the Tehri
dam (live storage about 2,590 Mm ) under construction; in addition, Kotli
Behl and Utyasu storage projects are at an advanced planning stage. These
three storage schemes are power/irrigation projects but water releases from
storage will probably be governed by hydro-power requirements.
2.11    Over the years, farmers in the upper third of the UGC system have
taken more than their fair share of water supplies and are growing substan-
tial areas of sugarcane (about 0.2 N ha). This inequitable water distribu-
tion has now become an established water right and is supported by a heavy
investment in sugar processing facilities. The water right cannot be changed
or corrected. Thus an essential objective of the rehabilitation of the UGC
system would be to generate additional water supplies for tail end users who
receive a poor irrigation service, in both qualitative and quantitative
terms. Additional water sources available within the UGC system would result
from:  (i) reduction of seepage losses in the unlined co veyance system which
at present averages about 2.6 cumecs/Mm (8.5 cusecs/Mft ) of wetted canal
surface; (ii) improvement in the operational efficiency; and (iii) develop-
ment of groundwater for conjunctive use with surface water supplies.
2.12    At present, operational losses are substantial since there are inade-
quate control structures to regulate and manage the flow. Once irrigation
starts, the system runs with little change in regime except occasionally to
respond to heavy rainfall. The ungated pipe outlets are easily tampered with
and do not provide constant flows or equitable distribution of water to the
areas served. Below the outlets, water 'istribution is inefficient since
the 'warabundi' system (para 5.09) cannot effectively function given the
condition of the watercourse channels. The unreliable timing and quality of
the irrigation service has resulted in a breakdown of basic discipline which
should be enforced by the Irrigation Department (ID). A number of outlet
studies in the UGC command area confirm that farmers at the head of the
system and near the outlets draw considerably more water than those less
favorably located.
2.13    Groundwater (Annex 8, SDV).  The project area is underlain by
alluvial deposits which form a single, though heterogenous, aquifer system
which can exceed 1,000 m in thickness in some parts. Aquifers are formed
by beds of sand which interfinger with beds of silt, clay and kankar. The
aquifer system is recharged mainly by rainfall, percolation from canals, and
field losses. Runoff is by seepage to the main natural drains which form
line-sinks for groundwater effluent. Depletion of groundwater also occurs
through evaporation from shallow water tables and by extraction by private



-9-
and public wells. Underflow due to the regional hydraulic gradient forms a
relatively small part of the water balance.
2.14    A comprehensive groundwater study of the UGC command area was under-
taken during project preparation. The mean annual recharge is estimated to
be about 6,200 MmW. Net extraction by various types of tubewells and dug-
wells amounted to about 3,800 Mm in 1980/81 or some 612 of the groundwater
resources. Thus, the prospect for further groundwater development in the
project area is good. Groundwater resources, estimated conservatively,
indicate that existing groundwater development and that proposed under the
project would still use only about 62% of exploitable amounts. Thus project
requirements of groundwater resources would be ensured.
2.15    Most of the water contained by the alluvial aquifer system has less
than 700 parts per million (ppm) dissolved solids. There are considerable
areas of better quality water with dissolved solids falling as low as
250 ppm. There are few pockets of water with high chloride concentrations
(in excess of 250 ppm Cl) but their localities are well defined and could be
avoided by project tubewells. As the pumped groundwater developed under the
project would be blended with surface water in the canals in proportions of
about 1:5, water quality problems are not foreseen.
Present ARricultural Development (Annex 9, SDV)
2.16    Crops.  The three crop seasons are the zaid (hot) season (roughly
from mid-March to the end of June), the kharif (wet) season (July-October),
and the rabi (dry) season (November-March). Apart from a small area under
orchards, the only perennial crop is sugarcane which occupies between 36% to
14% of the CCA in the five northern administrative districts (the sugarcane
zone) and 5% to 1% in the five southern districts. This pattern has emerged
because rainfall in the northern districts is higher, and the limited zaid
water supply, essential to sustain a perennial crop, is mainly diverted into
the upper part of the UGC scheme. The pattern is now institutionalized by
the extensive processing facilities in the northern districts. There is
greater uniformity in the area devoted to seasonal crops; irrigated wheat
averages about 30% of the CCA in the northern districts and about 26% in the
southern districts. Other rabi crops vary between 14% and 20%. Rice growing
increases inversely to the sugarcane area (8% in the north, 30% in the south)
and is the second choice for using kharif water. Other irrigated kharif
crops occupy 43% in the north and 16% in the south. Zaid (hot season) crop-
ping intensities are very low because of unreliable and limited water supply:
fodder, pulse crops and some maize are usually grown.
2.17    Farmers have only minimal fallow areas in both rabi and kharif
seasons. In kharif, they select a crop mix that enables them to plant almost
100% of their area either with a grain (pearl millet, sorghum and maize)
which usually receives little or no irrigation, or rice which has to be fully
irrigated and a fodder crop (sorghum, pulses). In rabi, by adjusting the
relative areas of wheat, barley and chickpeas (gram), farmers spread avail-
able water over most of their cultivable land. The only exception are areas
where infiltration rates are low and irrigation supplies are insufficient to
grow rice. These are the 'usar' lands, mostly occurring in the tail-end



-10-
distributary commands (para 2.04), and usually earmarked as village grazing
areas; usar lands can be readily reclaimed.
2.18    Crop Yields.  Present yield levels of principal crops, while showing
considerable variation between the better and less efficient farmers, compare
well to averages obtained in adjacent States:
Project Area     Puniab      Haryana
…                ----------- ton/ha…------    -
rice               2.1             2.9         2.7
wheat              2.1             2.7          2.3
sugarcane         43.0            56.5        40.0
At present rice generally receives lower investments per unit area, less atten-
tion to details of cultivation, and limited attention by research and extension
services.
Agricultural Supporting Services
2.19    Agricultural Research.  State Agricultural Universities are responsible
for basic and applied agricultural research; C.S. Pant Agricultural University
in Pantnagar and the C.S. Azad Agricultural University in Kanpur serve the
Meerut and Agra administrative divisions respectively.  Present research
programs cover all branches of agriculture but focus mainly on use of fer-
tilizer, pest control and crop varieties.  Notable achievements include
development and popularization of sugarcane varieties that continue to give
high recovery rates throughout an extended crushing season.  The Universities
pay less attention to water management and maximization of crop yields under
farmers' existing constraints of water availability. LimiCed work on cultural
practices has concentrated more on developing techniques suitable for trac-
tor-drawn than for animal-drawn equipment. The Department of Agriculture (DA)
is responsible for adaptive research and has ten regional agricultural testing
and demonstration stations throughout UP. The State also maintains an Irriga-
tion Research Institute at Roorke and a Horticultural Research Station at
Saharanpur. Central Government Research Institutes for sugarcane, grasslands
and soil conservation are located outside the project area at Lucknow, Jhansi
and Dehra Dun, respectively. Research facilities are currently being expanded
under the Bank supported National Agricultural Research Project (Cr. 855-IN,
US$27.OM).
2.20    Agricultural Extension.  Technically, agricultural extension is the
responsibility of the DA. However, the primary contacts with the farmers - the
multipurpose village level workers (VLW) - are under the administrative control
of the Block Development Officer of the Department of Community Development
(CD). On technical matters, VLWs receive guidance from staff of the District
Agriculture Office who are employees of the DA. In general, there are about 10
VLWs per block, covering about 25,000 to 30,000 ha (or one VLW for every 3,000
farm families). The present extension system is inadequate since: (i) exten-
sion services are poorly planned and supervised; (ii) VLWs have too many
responsibilities and are assigned to cover too large an area; (iii) linkages



-11-
with research and training are weak; and (iv) VLWs suffer from restricted
mobility and inadequate housing. The GOUP is aware of the shortcomings and
outdated nature of the existing extension service and has under active con-
sideration, the introduction of the Training and Visit (T&V) system (para
3.28).
2.21    Agricultural Inputs.  There is a well developed system for distribu-
tion and sale of fertilizers, pesticides and seeds throughout the project
area. Fertilizers are marketed mainly by the private sector; the DA sells
only about 1OX of the fertilizer used. Fertilizer consumption growth rate is
about 9% per annum and now exceeds 50 kg of nutrients per cropped ha. Pes-
ticide use is limited, with sugarcane and to a lesser extent rice as the main
users. The sales points are usually the same as for fertilizers and the
existing network would be adequate for the project needs. Improved seeds are
produced and marketed by the DA and by the Bank-supported Terai Seeds Cor-
poration (Ln. 614-IN; US$13.0 M). The main market is for hybrid pearl millet
seed and wheat. Some of the HYV rice seed sold by the cooperatives, usually
once or twice grown certified seed, is of poor quality.
2.22    Agricultural Credit.  Institutional credit organization follows the
normal pattern for India. There are about 1200 primary cooperative credit
societies and 54 branches of the State Land Development Bank (SLDB) within
the UGC command area. The cooperative banks, which provide short and medium
term loans mainly for agricultural inputs, are organized with the State
Cooperative Bank at the State level and 55 branch banks at the district
level. The SLDBs operate through their branches and affiliated offices in
%tehsils' (sub-district) and Block headquarters and handle long term credit
(up to 15 years). Commercial banks also have an important role in agricul-
tural lending (about 15Z). Despite this extensive credit network, growth in
actual cash terms has been negative over the last few years. Overdues of
cooperative banks are presently about one-quarter of the demand and statewide
recovery performance of the SLDB in 1981-82 was about 66%. In general, there
is no serious credit constraints but the recovery performance give grounds
for concern.
2.23    Farm Power.  There are about 5,000 tractors and nearly one million
pair of oxen and draft buffaloes in the project area, or about one HP of
draft power per ha of cultivable land. Tractor sales are in excess of
machines being scrapped. The condition of the animals is adequate because of
ample fodder production and the large amount of available stover and straw.
Therefore, there is not likely to be a draft power shortage within the
project area. About 200,000 private tubewells (PTWs) use the easily
exploitable shallow aquifers and are an important contribution to the area's
productivity. About two-thirds of the PTWs are in the southern one-third of
the area, principally due to the inadequate and erratic canal water supplies.
The wells are used mainly in the rabi and zaid seasons but also provide
supplementary water supply during kharif. The motors of PTWs provide an
important source of power for other farm activities such as threshing.
2.24    Marketinx. Processing and Storage.  Marketing in UP is regulated
under the Agricultural Produce Market Act (1964); marketable surplus is
traded in main wholesale markets and sub-yards throughout the project area.



-12-
In general, marketing facilities have matched the increasing crop production
for most farm products. Foodgrains in excess of intra-State demand are
procured by GOI agencies or bought by private traders. Oilseed crops and
pulses find a ready market; the constraint to expanding trade is the limited
supply, not demand. Processing facilities are adequate for all the common
crops grown. There are 26 industrial sugarmills and nearly 800 jaggery
(khandasari) mills, mainly located in the sugarcane zone. Flour milling and
rice husking are well developed both as a cottage and large-scale industry.
The standard of rice husking is good, especially for processing the
high-value, long-grained scented (Basmati) rice. Storage, which is adequate
in both the public and private sector, is provided by three main agencies:
the State Warehousing Corporation; the Central Warehousing Corporation; and
the State Marketing Federation. In addition, some cooperatives also have
godowns. The Bank Group is presently financing The Second Fooodgrain Storage
Project (Cr. 747-IN; US$107 M) in UP (among other States in India) and
another storage project is being financed by the European Economic Community.
III. THE PROJECT
Proiect Formulation
3.01    By the mid 1970s, GOUP had recognized that the UGC system was reach-
ing the end of its effective life span. The original planning and design
objectives of the scheme could not be adapted to meet requirements of modern
irrigated agriculture without major rehabilitation. Furthermore, an impor-
tant inequity in water supply has developed through extensive sugarcane
cultivation in the upper part of the project area. This has made the irriga-
tion service to the lower part of the command even more unreliable and
untimely because of the reduced water supply. Since all the dependable water
resources in the river Ganga have been committed in western UP, GOUP has
recognized that the only way to provide additional supplies is from within
existing canal command areas. To reduce the present inequities, GOUP has
decided that water supplies resulting from savings or additions would be
passed down the system to the areas which are under-supplied at present.
3.02    Preliminary project preparation was carried out by the technical
departments of GOUP in close coordination with the Central Water Commission
(CWC) of GOI. In August 1979, GOI and GOUP decided to approach the Bank
regarding a possible UP Composite Irrigation Project to modernize the UGC,
LGC and Agra Canal systems. A consultancy firm, Water and Power Consultancy
Services (India) Ltd., (WAPCOS), New Delhi, India, was retained to prepare
the Project Feasibility Report. An Identification Report was produced in
November 1980, followed by a comprehensive UGC Feasibility Report in December
1982. On the basis of these reports, it was decided to restrict the proposed
project to the UGC system and also to exclude the main canal headworks, so
that the project would be strictly a rehabilitation and modernization opera-
tion and not a new diversion scheme.
3.03    Project Obiectives.  The main project objectives are (i) risk aver-
sion; (ii) rehabilitation and modernization of the system and using the



-13-
savings resulting from lining canals and improvements in the operational
characteristics of the canal system to increase the commanded area;
(iii) supplement water supply through conjunctive use of groundwater with
surface water supplies; and (iv) improvement of the State's capability to
plan, design and manage irrigation schemes. An additional objective of the
first time slice is to maximize benefits from the sunk investment in the
Madhya Ganga (I) Canal system, which physically interrelates with the UGC
system (para 2.10). Agricultural development needed to realize project
benefits have been identified and would be implemented outside the project
(para 3.28). An agreement has been reached with GOUP that it would carry out
the planning, design, construction, and operation and maintenance of the
project works in accordance with standards and criteria acceptable to IDA.
3.04    Justification for Bank Involvement.  The proposed project will be
the first in the State which attempts a comprehensive rehabilitation and
modernization of a major existing irrigation system. The technologies and
implementation procedures will be novel since, in addition to a major con-
struction program, the existing irrigation service must be maintained to
protect present benefits. The first time slice will demonstrate how this can
be done. It will also have important sectoral implications since there are
several other major canals system in UP which need to be modernized (para
3.02). In addition, the project will assist the Irrigation Department (ID)
to develop its capabilities for planning, design, monitoring and evaluation
of completed works both in terms of quantity and quality of irrigation serv-
ice and agricultural benefits. In this respect, Bank involvement during the
project preparation stage has already resulted in considerable cost savings
through more effective planning and design. It has also focussed Govern-
ment's attention on operation and maintenance of existing works which, except
for establishment costs, are considerably underfunded. The most important
justification for Bank involvement in the project is to emphasize the
priority that GOUP must give to rehabilitation and modernization of existing
irrigation schemes if the State's present irrigation potential is to be
translated into agricul:ural benefits. Although the priority had been recog-
nized at the technical and administrative levels of Government, allocation of
scarce development budget to existing irrigated areas has had, until this
project, little priority politically since it is more attractive to propose
and start new irrigation schemes.
Water SuDplP and Demand
3.05    The water supply for the UGC scheme is obtained from run-of-the river
flows which are subject to seasonal and annual variations. Surface water
availability, calculated from long-term streamflow records (Annex 7, SDV),
has therefore been based on the 75% dependable flow in river Ganga estimated
for each cropping season. Groundwater availability has been estimated using
a comprehensive groundwater model which was completed during project prepara-
tion (para 2.14). Crop water requirements have been calculated in accordance
with FAO Publication No. 24 utilizing the modified Penman method of estimat-
ing potential evapotranspiration and related to expected cropping patterns
for the project's various agro-climatic zones. Water balance studies take
into account the historical right of the UGC system to divert water from the
river Ganga, effective rainfall, reliable groundwater supplies, and the



-14-
estimated demand for irrigation, domestic and industrial water supplies.
These studies show that potential irrigation demand will always exceed supply
within the UGC command area, except for a short period in the kharif season
(July to September) when the river is in flood. Thus conjunctive use of
groundwater within the UGC scheme will be important, since it will
(i) enable variations inherent in existing surface water supply to be
reduced; (ii) help alleviate local groundwater drainage problems where they.
exist; and (iii) in particular, complement insufficient surface supplies for
the rice crop (especially for rice nurseries) which occur before river flows
increase significantly in June/July.
3.06    In addition to the UGC system, there are (existing, under construc-
tion or planned) five other major canals, with a combined diversion capacity
of about 43,000 cusecs along the stretch of the Ganga River that forms a
boundary of the UGC command area. Historical flow data indicate that there
is insufficient dependable flow in the river to satisfy all these projects
through a complete kharif season, specially before July when rice is to be
planted and in the last few weeks before its maturing after September. Thus,
the project could be affected in those low flow years when GOUP has to decide
how to distribute a shortfall in water supply between the various canal
systems that interrelate with the UGC scheme. Within the UGC system, the
project aims to distribute equitably water supplies throughout the command
area. To achieve this, water savings or additions obtained through project
works would be passed down the canal system to make up water shortages in the
tail reaches of the command below the sugarcane zone. In addition, blocks to
be modernized would be designed with a water allowance per unit of cultivable
area assuming the full modernization of the UGC scheme. This would avoid
farmers in the first modernized blocks establishing a 'users right' to excess
water allocation, similar to existing privileged farmers in the sugarcane
zone; such a right would be difficult to take away when the development
proceeds and extra water developed by the project is needed elsewhere in the
UGC command. With respect to water allocation both to and within the UGC
system, GOUP has undertaken that it would (i) allocate to the UGC system the
right to water supplies from the Ganga as assumed in project planning and
design; (ii) allocate water supplies equitably within the project command;
and (iii) allocate to modernized blocks under the project, water supplies per
unit area as shall be required to irrigate such modernized blocks as
designed, assuming that the entire UGC system is modernized.
Proiect Scope
3.07    The project would finance a six-year time slice to rehabilitate and
modernize the UGC system. The entire development program is likely to take
at least 30 years to complete, depending on finance resources available to
GOUP and the relative priorities of other works in the State's irrigation
sector. Project planning has been confined at present to a first phase
development (about 18 years) which is within reasonably predictable planning
horizons. The work program, which includes expensive common works such as
the replacement main canal, has been scheduled to give maximum
benefit/investment returns. In addition, the >roject has been formulated
with maximum flexibility so that GOUP can eva.aate and amend, if necessary,



-15-
the level of modernization which would be appropriate and affordable, given
statewide priorities in the irrigation sector.
Project Components
3.08    The principal components are:
(a)  Risk Aversion Works.  Replacement of the four major cross
drainage structures on the main canal together with construction
of lined channel to link these structures back to the existing
canal (mile 3-22), and construction of a lined channel parallel
to the existing main canal common to both the UGC and MGC sys-
tems (mile 118-148).  Works would include lining about 4.0 Mm2
of canal surface, and improvement, replacement and modernization
of canal structures and regulation equipment;
(b) Modernization of Canals. Initial works for the replacement of
the existing main canal (mile 148-181) by a parallel lined
channel together with replacement of regulation, access and
other canal structures; modernization of the canal systems in
three distributary comoands serving about 43,000 ha including
lining of about 1.6 Mm of canal surface on some 140 km of
distributary and minor canals and replacement and upgrading of
regulating, access and escape structures;
(c)  Modernization of Watercourses.  Lining and improvement of struc-
tures for watercourses totalling about 1.5 M running meters and
serving about 43,000 ha;
(d) AuRmentation Tubewells. Installation and electrification of
about 33 augmentation tubewells tATW) with a total capacity of
about 2.2 cumecs on distributary canals to be modernized;
{e) Canal Communication System. Installation of two-way radio and
teleprinter networks to improve the efficiency of the canal
commun-cation system;
(f)  Drainage Improvements.  A reconnaissance drainage study of the
UGC command area and a detailed drainage study with pilot
program of drainage works for the three distributary command
areas to be modernized;
(g) Hydro-electric (Hydel) Stations. Replacement of Palra hydel
station by a new station on the parallel main canal (about mile
148.6) and initiation of the civil works for a replacement for
Pathari hydel station at mile 7 on the main canal;
(h) Water and Land Management Institute. Setting up and initial
operation of a Water and Land Management Institute (WALMI);



-16-
(i) Management Improvements. Construction of residential buildings
for Irrigation Department staff; provision of facilities, serv-
ices, study tours and visits for project management staff;
in-service training and technical assistance.
(j)  Vehicles, Plant and Equipment.  Procurement of the vehicles,
plant and equipment for construction and management of the
project works;
Detailed Description
3.09    Risk Aversion Works (Annex 15, SDV).  Under the project, priority has
been given to risk aversion works to protect the existing benefits from
irrigation. The greatest risk to the existing system dre four major
cross-drainage structures on torrent streams over the' wtin canal between mile
5 and 18, located upstream of the first major offtate from the main canal
(mile 22). In recent years, the structures have shown signs of deterioration
and the possibility and effects of failure are such that they should be
replaced as soon as possible. The highest priorities for replacement are
Solani aqueduct and Dhanori level crossing (Table 2a, Annex 3). The replace-
ment structures are located so that most of the existing main canal would
have to be replaced to provide approach and exit alignments to the new struc-
ture sites. In addition, the main canal would be designed (i) with increased
capacity (294 to 350 cumecs) to operate a new silt ejector at about mile 13;
(ii) to give a least cost cross section since it need not be restricted by
design levels at existing structures; and (iii) so that the existing main
canal can be operated almost continuously. The above considerations will be
taken into account when finalizing the design for the replacement canal on
this upper reach. Replacement canal reaches will be lined.
3.10    To reduce the risk to the UGC system while works described above are
under construction, the main canal common to both UGC and MGC systems (mile
118 to 148) would be rehabilitated and new lined parallel canal would be
constructed on this reach to complement the capacity of the existing unlined
main canal (Map IBRD 17648R). The new lined channel (about 140 cumecs at
mile 118) would carry all rabi season flows to the UGC system. The existing
unlined main canal would be operated in kharif when peak design discharges
occur. This will effectively eliminate risk to the UGC system below mile 118
(about 20Z of UGC command area) since this part of the system could be
provided with an alternative diversion source from the river Ganga. It will
also enable the existing main canal (mile 118-148) to become an important
section of feeder canal for the MGC system and remove the existing capacity
constraint within the UG canal above mile 148. Between mile 22 and 118, risk
of failure on the main canal is small and vould mainly consist of the pos-
sible failure of cross channel structures. Under general maintenance funds
for the UGC scheme, GOUP would rehabilitate or replace any failure as tem-
porary works until the main canal between mile 22-118 is replaced by a new
lined channel.
3.11    Main Canal Modernization.  Work done on the main canal described
under risk aversion works would include a modernization element. Work would
also be initiated under the project on a lined parallel replacement channel



-17-
between mile 148 and 181. The replacement and parallel channels would be
designed so that water savings from lining and additional supplies from ATWs
could be conveyed to the lower part of the UGC command area. The replacement
canal would be lined to reduce seepage losses and maintenance needs and
sufficient control structures would be provided so that the system can
respond adequately and efficiently to changing seasonal water demands. The
new channel would replace rather than complement the existing main canal
since this would (i) avoid costs associated with linking the unlined and
lined channels at common control points; (ii) reduce channel section since
the new design would not have to be compatible with the existing unlined
channel; and (iii) eliminate costs of rehabilitating many structures on the
existing main canal which would need to be replaced if the existing canal was
to continue to function. 1/ A flexible approach of staged construction would
be adopted for channels with capacities above 40 cumecs so that they can be
enlarged in the future. Improved control and adequate escapes would enable
encroachment on lined freeboard for short periods of peak demand.
3.12    The type of lining used on the main canal (and branch canals under
future work programs) would depend on channel capacity, availability of
materials and construction methods appropriate to the site. Generally,
lining would be burnt brick tiles with a single layer on the bed set in
cement plaster, and a double layer of tiles on side slopes set in cement
plaster with a sandwiched layer of cement plaster. For both bed and side
slopes, the tile plaster bed layer would be placed over a compacted subgrade.
Cross regulators on the main canal would be provided with either radial or
lifting gates (depending on canal capacity) with gearing mechanism and
designed for eventual mechanization with electric motors. Access structures
would be designed to accommodate modern loading factors and re-sited, where
necessary, to reflect present and future needs.
3.13    Canal Distribution System Modernization.  The irrigation system below
the main canal consist of branch canals, distributaries, minors and vater-
courses. Typically, a distributary commands 12,000-25,000 ha, though there
are many canals designated as distributaries outside this range. The dis-
tributaries feed minors, each serving about 200-1,000 ha. There are
presently ungated pipe outlets to chaks (outlet commands) on all elements
of the canal system including the main canal down to minors. Chak areas
range from one hectare to over 200 ha with about 90Z between 30-60 ha.
Watercourses (guls) deliver irrigation water to field channels within the
chaks. Thus modernization of the distribution system would include: (i)
rationalization of the various canal command area elements; (ii) elimination
of direct outlets from main, branch and major distributary canals, where
possible; (iii) lining of canals to reduce seepage losses; (iv) provision of
improved regulation structures to control water delivery within the system;
and (v) provision of escape structures.
1/ The right-of-way of the existing main canal may be used at some future
date to channelize supplies to the Lower Ganga Canal (LGC) rather than
pass valuable rabi water down the river Ganga to headworks of the LGC
system at Narora Barrage (Map IERD, 17648R).



-18-
3.14    Design criteria for the canal distribution systems are discussed in
Annex 10, SDV. Canal distribution system modernization would be undertaken
in Bulandshar and Harduaganj distributary commands serving about 39,000 ha,
and initiated in Machua distributary command (about 4,000 ha). Dis-
tributaries and minors would be lined, except for limited areas where lining
can be proved unnecessary as a result of tests and observations (e.g., imper-
vious soils, etc.). Generally, existing channels would be modernized and
lined except when extended closures of the canal system may not practical; in
such cases, a parallel lined canal would be constructed. Lining would be
either pre-cast concrete slabs or, more commonly, a single layer of burnt
clay tiles bedded in cement mortar, placed on a compacted sub-grade. The
cement plaster layer is the seepage barrier with tiles providing mechanical
protection. Where appropriate, mechanical protection may be provided by
pre-cast concrete slabs and a polyethyelene film may be substituted for the
cement plaster layer. Field testing of various types of lining would con-
tinue throughout the project period and specifications would be modified if
improvements are recognized, or a more cost-effective design proves to be
practical. Since burnt clay tiles vould be the preferred lining material,
about five improved automatic tile kilns with modern tile moldiog equipment
would be set up under the project. This would enable high quality clay tiles
to be manufactured to uniform standards.
3.15    Improved water control and regulation capabilities are essential for
more efficient management of the UGC System. Existing control structures are
insufficient and do not allow the water regulation required for efficient and
timely distribution and delivery of irrigation supplies. In addition, most
structures are in poor condition and many have reached the end of their
useful life. Project modernization vorks would include replacement of
regulators on all canals, construction of additional cross regulators,
installation of water measuring devices in head reaches of larger channels,
remodelling of watercourse outlets, provision for escapes as needed, and
remodelling of falls, as well as construction of ancillary facilities includ-
ing cow ghats, bridges, and buildings. Design of water regulation and con-
trol facilities would provide for future upgrading through introduction of
improved technologies for canal control.
3.16    Costs for canal modernization including structures, staff support
(such as housing units for construction field staff), engineering, but
excluding physical and price contingencies, average about Rs 5,200 per ha
CCA, including lining estimated at Rs 2,100 per ha CCA.
3.17    Watercourse Modernization.  The proposed project would finance mod-
ernization (mainly lining) of about 950 watercourses within the distributary
block comands to be modernized under the project (para 3.14). After
rationalization of chak commands, each watercourse system would serve on
average about 50 farmers, linking individual fasm plots within an area of
about 8 ha to the canal network operated by the Government. The linkage from
the lined watercourse to the farmer's field would be tbrough unlined field
channels. Typically, a watercourse system is about 1.7 km in length and has
a capacity of about 1.0 cusecs. In general, water losses are highest in



-19-
watercourse head reaches, because the head reach runs longer than the tail
reach due to operation of the warabundi rotation (para 5.09).
3.18    Watercourse modernization would follow designs which have proven
effective and economical in Bank-supported projects elsewhere in India. The
lining for an individual watercourse would average about 75% of its total
length and, depending on local costs and site conditions, would be either:
(i) vertical sides constructed of brick masonry on an in-situ concrete slab
bed, or (ii) vertical brick masonry sides as above, but with brick masonry
bed over a compacted subgrade. In very sandy areas, the inside face of the
brick masonry lining would have cement mortar plaster to minimize leakage,
and polyethylene sheet would be used in the bed as an additional seepageF
barrier. On about one per cent of the watercourse l-ning program, innovative
designs and materials would be tried and results monitored and integrated
into the on-going program. Watercourse modernization would also include
construction of improved gated turnouts, division boxes, culverts,
cross-drainage structures and falls. Before a modernized watercourse is
considered complete, performance of each modernized watercourse would be
monitored for six months to ensure design specifications are net; any repairs
or modifications needed would be made. After completion, the lined
watecourse would be maintained by the ID. The average cost of watercourse
modernization, excluding physical and price contingencies, is estimated at
about Rs 53 per running metre.
3.19    Augmentation Tubewells (ATWs).  With full modernization of the UGC
system, ATWs would be constructed along main, branch and selected dis-
tributary channels and grouped so that (i) they can be served by separate or
"dedicated" 11 kV feeder lines from the nearest 132 kV sub-station or canal
hydel station; and (ii) the combined discharge would be large enough to be
operationally significant when used conjunctively with surface water supplies
in the canal system, but would not exceed 20X of surface water capacity in
the parent canal.
3.20    Under the project, about 33 ATWs with a total capacity of about
2.2 cumecs would be installed along Bulandshahr and Harduaganj dis-
tributaries, and spaced to deliver about 3 cusecs per 1.5 mile reach of canal
in a single row discharging directly into the canals.  Minimum spacing
between ATWs and private tubewells would be about 150m. Each ATW would
consist of a well, submersible pump unit, switch gear, starter and protection
relay, delivery pipework, and power lines (Annex 12, SDV). Project ATWs
would be served by about 81 km of dedicated 11 kV feeder lines. Capacitors
and separate switch gear would be provided on 11 kV lines at each sub-station
to improve the voltage at the feeder end. The cost of typical 1.5 and 3.0
cusecs ATWs, excluding physical and price contingencies, would be about Rs.
0.22 M and Rs. 0.29 M respectively. Agreements have been reached with GOUP
that it would cause UPSEB to: (i) provide independent electric 11 kV feeders
from 132 kV sub-stations to connect all ATWs; (ii) ensure that these feeders
would remain exclusive to the ATWs; (iii) provide ATWs with continuous power
supply when required with a voltage fluctuation of not greater than +6%; and
(iv) energize the tubewell system according to a schedule to be prepared by
ID not later than September 30, 1986 and acceptable to IDA.



-20-
3.21    Canal Communication System (Annex 13. SDV).  The present canal comr-
munication system uses telegraph and magneto (local communication) telephones
to connect the major regulation and control points and has been in operation
since the beginning of the century. Modern and dependable communications are
required to improve canal system operations and management efficiency. As a
first step, the project would provide two-way radio sets for voice communica-
tion between the most important stations in the UCC and MGC systems. Sta-
tions to be upgraded are located at regulation points on main canals, impor-
tant cross-drainage points and locations involving inter-scheme regulators
which supply the Agra and Lower Ganga Canal systems. The GOI Post and
Telegraphs (PMT) Department would provide base station two-way radio sets for
rent or purchase, which would be housed at existing communications facilities
and operated by the present signalling staff. The project would also provide
for (i) additional hand-held units on a pilot demonstration basis; and (ii)
services of a local consultant to assist with detailed planning of the canal
communications network to maximize system management and operational
efficiency. GOUP has completed a feasibility study of the required UGC
communication system and final allocation of frequency bands will be based on
the results of this study. The total cost for the complete communication
network for the fully modernized system is estimated at Rs. 69.3 M of which
equipment costing about Rs. 16.1 M would be installed over the project
period. An agreement has been reached with GOI that radio frequencies for
the UGC communication system would be allocated to GOUP by August 31, 1985.
3.22    Drainage Improvements.  There are no major sub-surface drainage
requirements within the distributary block commands to be modernized under
the project. However, reconnaisance surveys in Aligarh and Bulandshar dis-
tricts indicate that drainage would be needed within the UGC command to
remove excess surface water. Local flooding can occur because natural
drainage channels have been interrupted by physical obstacles such as roads,
bunds and irrigation channels and have tended to silt up. As a consequence,
the natural waterways need to be remodelled and enlarged so that drainage
effluent can be removed from the cultivated area before standing crops are
damaged. In addition, the Lower Ganga Canal (LGC) forms a barrier across the
tail end of the UGC command area (Map IBRD 17648R) and an outfall drain,
parallel to the LGC, will be necessary to remove drainage surplus to the
River Yanuma once more irrigated water supplies reach the southern part of
UGC command. Thoughout the UGC command, minor drainage channels would be
required to effectively collect rainfall runoff and to provide outlets for
canal escapes. Link drains would be provided where necessary to remove
runoff to the nearest drainage ways, which would be enlarged and remodelled
to remove bottlenecks. Cost estimates have been provisionally based on
drainage ditches with capacities of about 1.0 1/sec/ha. Under the project, a
detailed drainage study would be undertaken for the command areas of the
three distributaries to be modernized under this time slice (64,000 ha).
Based on the results of the study, the project would support a pilot drainage
work program which would include remodelling and enlarging the natural
drainage waterways, construction of collector drainage channels to field
drain level, all structures required for access, and initial construction on
works required to remove drainage surplus from the UGC command. In addition,
a reconnaissance level survey would be undertaken to prepare a general
drainage plan for the entire UGC command.



-21-
3.23    Hydro-electric-(Hydel) Stations (Annex 11, SDV).  There are eight
generating stations on the existing UG main canal with an installed capacity
of about 45 MW. The earliest stations were commissioned in the 1930s and all
units are reaching the end of their useful life. As the main canal is mod-
ernized, new power houses will be constructed on the replacement parallel
channel at or near the existing generating stations. Under the project, the
Palra station (0.5 MW) would be replaced at about mile 148.6 on the main
canal (Map IBRD, 17648R). In addition, construction would commence on civil
works of the Pathari station (27 MW) located at mile 7 on the main canal.
The stations would be multi-purpose structures utilizing the controlled fall
as a regulating point on the main canal and also providing road access over
the canal. The project would: {i) finance civil works undertaken by the ID
in conjunction with the canal construction program; and (ii) procurement and
installation of mechanical and electrical equipment and switchyards to con-
nect the stations to the grid through UPSEB.
3.24    Water and Land ManaRement Institute (WALMI).  As part of a long-term
program to build up the professional cadre for irrigation management in the
State, GOUP would commence in-service training programs in water management
and land development for irrigated agriculture (Annex 14, SDV). The project
would construct a new training center with residential and training accom-
modation at Okhla near New Delhi (Map IBRD 17648R). Provision would be made
for equipment, incremental staff salaries and training allowances, consultant
services, and to a limited extent, training visits for institute staff both
within India and abroad. The GOUP has issued the order to sanction the WAIMI
and posted the first WALMI Director. Agreements have been reached with GOUP
that it would Ci) maintain WALNI headed by a Director with suitable
qualifications and experience; and (ii) by September 30, 1985, draw up a
student training program acceptable to IDA and start such training by March
31, 1986.
3.25    Management Improvements.  Both residential and non-residential build-
ings are provided as standard items under all construction programs vith the
number and size of the buildings based on long established statewide norms.
The buildings used during construction are handed over to O&M staff when
works are commissioned; non-residential buildings are used for management
purposes and storage facilities for O&M supplies and equipment. Although
these arrangements are satisfactory for construction and O&M staff and are
provided for under the project as part of civil works, there has been no
systematic provision for the other ID staff. In recent years, investigation
and planning (I&P), design and monitoring and evaluation activities have been
decentralized from Lucknow as the statewide development program has expanded.
In most regional and divisional headquarters, the ID has very little accom-
modation and rentals are expensive and limited. As a consequence, par-
ticulaxly in western UP, the ID has difficulty in attracting qualified staff,
for essential ID activities other than construction and O&M. The ID has
recognized that rehabilitation and modernization of existing irrigation
systems will continue in western UP (para 3.04) at least on a scale equal to
the project into the foreseeable future. Therefore, under the project,
residential buildings would be constructed for selected grades of staff in
I&P and design activities (para 5.06) so that about 66% of staff required for



-22-
the project would be housed by project completion. The buildings would be
standard ID designs and generally constructed on land presently owned by the
ID. The type and size of houses, unit costs, and the expenditure schedule
are given in Table 9, Annex 3. The project would also provide funds for (i)
special studies by ID staff or consultants or services from universities and
other research institutions in India, and (ii) equipment and vehicles for
project preparation, implementation and management. In addition, the project
would provide for study tours to enable ID staff to visit other projects both
within India and abroad, to widen their professional experience. Detailed
costs of these items is given in Tables 10 and 11, Annex 3.
3.26    Technical Services.  During project preparation, GOUP has benefited
(and will continue to benefit) from implementation assistance which brings
outside ideas and new methodology into the ID. Furthermore, the ID is
staffed almost exclusively with engineers and has limited experience in
planning work that requires a multi-disciplinary approach. It would also
benefit from an advisory input with respect to operation, maintenance and
general management of completed irrigation schemes. The ID is aware of the
areas were improvements can be made and has indicated willingness to consider
technical assistance under the project and to assist in planning further
development stages after completion of the first time slice.
3.27    The project would finance the cost of a Advisory Planning Group (APG)
which could include either qualified Indian or International Consultants (or
a joint venture). The APG would assist the ID in those tasks which cannot be
fully or adequately undertaken within the Department. This would include
agricultural and socio-economic studies and planning requirements, techniques
for effective monitoring and data collection; and evaluation of data col-
lected, supervision of baseline and other special surveys; and macro planning
and design of subsequent phases of the UGC modernization program. The APG
would also advise the ID on operation, maintenance and general management of
the UGC system.  The consultancy service would involve about 154 man months.
The monthly cost including salaries, fees and subsistence for foreign con-
sultants would average about US$8,700. Detailed terms of reference of APG
are given in Annex 4 and the estimated costs (assuming foreign consultants
are retained)-are summarized in Table 10, Annex 3.  An agreement has been
reached with GOUP that by December 31, 1984, it would retain Consultants to
provide general assistance to the ID in accordance with terms of reference
agreed with IDA and with experience and qualifications acceptable to IDA.
Agricultural Extension and Research
3.28    Agricultural extension in the project area, and applied research to
address farmer's needs under the project need considerable strengthening. In
this respect, GOUP are already engaged (with Bank's assistance) in preparing
an agricultural extension project based on the Training and Visit (T&V)
system. In addition, the on-going Bank-supported National Agricultural
Research Project (Cr. IN-855; US$ 27.0 M) provides the means to finance
apprcpriate applied research programs; though funds provided under this
project are already fully committed, it is expected that the Bank will con-
tinue to support applied research in India through a follow-up project.
Should such IDA financing not become available, the applied research program



-23-
required for the project would be financed by GOUP. Therefore, instead of
including agricultural extension and research components in the project, an
agreement has been reached with GOUP that, by December 31, 1986, it would
strengthen, as required, the existing organization and service such that it
can adequately perform agricultural extension activities in the Bulandshahr
and Aligarh administrative districts of Uttar Pradesh. Agreements have also
been reached with GOUP that it would: (i) not later than December 1, 1984,
prepare a proposal for an applied agricultural research program acceptable to
IDA, for submission to the Indian Council of Agricultural Research (ICAR) for
its review and comments; (ii) commence applied research investigations not
later than June 1, 1985; and (iii) review and update the program annually
commencing on March 1, 1986.
Status of Project Preparation
3.29    The project works are in close proximity to existing channels and
structures for which the ID has as-built drawings. Therefore the project
preparation is based on accurate site data including long and cross sections
for channels and foundation conditions for most structures. Where necessary,
ID is complementing available data with an on-going site investigation
program, particularly for sections of the main canal and some major struc-
tures which are designed as individual works. Works below branch canal head
regulators are prepared using type designs for channels and structures
adapted to suit actual site conditions. The ID has prepared the type designs
necessary for project implementation based on design principles and criteria
formulated during project preparation and approved by IDA. Detailed layouts
have been prepared for the distribution blocks to be modernized under the
project (para 3.13). The status of preparation of the project is reflected
in the physical contingencies for civil works which vary from about 7% to 15%
and is sufficient to estimate project costs with acceptable accuracy. The ID
has made provisions satisfactory to IDA to ensure that detailed designs will
be available in a timely manner to meet the project implementation schedule.
3.30    Of the major works, the four cross-drainage structures on the main
canal (para 3.09) are under design based on similar structures have been
built recently elsewhere in UP; the ID expects to complete the detailed
designs by about August 1985 for review by IDA. Construction of the first
major structure will start in March 1986 and the remainder will start at
intervals up to March 1990. Detailed designs have been completed for about
4,000 ha of the distributary blocks to be modernized and which are scheduled
to be under construction by September 1985. Additional surveys required to
finalize layouts of other areas to be modernized under the project will be
completed by December 1985.
Implementation Schedule (Chart C-2)
3.31    The project would be implemented over six years from September, 1984
to September, 1990. The main construction season is from September to May
although certain activities, particularly on major works, can continue
throughout the year. In many new projects in India, utilization of water has
been slow because minor distribution systems were not constructed ful'ly or



-24-
concurrently with water being available in major canals. To avoid this risk,
the distribution systems would be modernized on a block-by-block basis con-
currently with completion of the main distributary channel. Construction
work would be scheduled with a minimum interruption of water deliveries to
existing irrigation schemes. Farmers will be informed of canal closures at
least one crop season ahead to enable them to make suitable adjustments in
their cropping patterns.

April 1984 contract prices. The provision for physical contingencies range
between 5% and 17% depending on the status of designs and average about 9%
for all civil works. Engineering, design and administration are estimated at
about 13% of the civil works including local costs of engineering services
retained by the ID for investigation, planning and design (para 3.27).
Expected price increases during the project period are taken as 7.0 % in
1984/85, 8.0 % in 1985/87, and 6.0% thereafter.

Financing
4.03      The proposed IDA credit of US$125.0 M, would finance the foreign
exchange component (about US$26.3 M) and about 48% of local costs. All
expenditures would be met by GOUP from its development budget, which includes
a GOI contribution. The IDA credit would be made to GOI on standard IDA
terms. An agreement has been reached that GOI would make the proceeds of the
credit available to GOUP on GOI's standard terms and arrangements for
development assistance to the States. In addition, agreements have been
reached that from GOUP, it would promptly provide resources and funds to (i)
execute the project in accordance with the implementation and expenditure
schedules for the project period, and (ii) operate and maintain adequately
and efficiently the irrigation systems under the project.
