Hj Excluding USS2.4 H which is unallocated to specific work. (see Table T-5).
4.05      Works to be financed under the project would cost approximately US$
167.2 M, excluding equipment (para 4.06) and engineering and administration.
About US$ 87.1 M, or 52% of the civil works, would be let through Interna-
tional Competitive Bidding (ICB) contracts, about US$57.4 N (34Z) through
Local Competitive Bidding (LCB) contracts, and the remaining US$22.7 M (14%)
would be let under piecework contracts or be carried out by GOUP's departmen-
tal forces. All ICB and LCB contracts for civil works would be let on the
basis of standardized documents and procedures recently developed by GOI's
Central Water Commission and approved by the Bank Group.



-26-
(a) ICB Contracts for Main Canal Works (US$87.1 M). Works for main
canal in excess of US$6 M per contract would be let on the basis
of ICB in accordance with the Bank Group's procurement
guidelines. To encourage the participation of larger contrac-
tors, similar works would be packaged where practical for bid-
ding. However, to gain the interest of smaller contractors, the
bid package would be designed also to allow bidding for all or
any part of the work. Indian contractors would be entitled to a
7.5% preference for the purpose of bid evaluation. Tender
documents would specify this preference and the manner of its
application.
(b) LCB Contracts (US$55.9 M). These works would be individually
small (less then US$6 M per contract), scattered over a large
area, and carried out intermittently as determined by weather
conditions and agricultural activities. They would therefore
not be suitable for international tendering.
Cc)  Advance Contracting (US$1.5 M).  Works contracts will have been
let between January 1984 when the project was appraised and
September 1984 when the time-slice covered by the project is
expected to start. These contracts are not suitable for ICB
for the same reasons as described in item (b) above and would be
let after LCB on the basis of CWC's standardized procedures and
guidelines. An estimated US$1.5 M worth of works would be
outstanding under such contracts after September 1984 and would
be eligible for financing from the proposed credit.
Cd) Non-ICB/LCB Works (US$22.7 M). These works would also be
individually small and scattered and their implementation would
be governed by weather and agricultural conditions. Because of
these and other technical constraints, there would be a general
lack of interest from contractors lozated outside the areas
covered by the project. These works would therefore be imprac-
tical for ICB or country-wide tendering; most of them would
instead be carried out under small piece-work or rate contracts.
When required by safety or quality considerations or when the
quantities of work are difficult to estimate in advance, small
works may be implemented by GOUP's departmental forces.
4.06    Power Supplv.  Power transmission and transiormation works and
procurement and installation of electrical and mechanical equipment in the
hydel stations to be financed under the project would cost approximately
US$0.4 M and US$1.4 M respectively, excluding engineering and administration.
As with IDA credits for rural electrification and small hydel installations,
UPSEB would be responsible for the works including procurement of its own
project materials, using Rural Electrification Corporation (REC) standard
specifications and standard General Conditions of Contract approved by IDA.
Generally, the UPSEB would not procure materials and equipment for project
works as separate contracts but include the project items as part of their
regular procurement for the statewide rural electrification programs. In
view of the expected large number of small contracts and the present smooth



-27-
functioning of the procurement process, only contracts exceeding US$0.5 K
equivalent would require IDA approval prior to contract awards. The UPSEB
procurement procedures for materials procured without using Credit proceeds
are satisfactory.
4.07    Equipment, Instruments and Vehicles (Table 11, Annex 3) to be
financed under the project would cost about US$12.8 M, excluding engineering
and administration cost and goods required for power components (para 4.06).
This includes (i) about US$6.9 K for light and heavy equipment required for
civil works (para 4.05), procured by GOUP and provided by the ID to contrac-
tors; (ii) about US$2.5 M for ATWs, WALMI and the canal communication sys-
tem; and (iii) about US$3.4 M for management improvements. Of this equip-
ment, about US$5.8 e would be suitable for procurement through ICB procedures
and US$5.2 M would be procured through LCB procedures. In addition, about
US$1.8 M of equipment, instruments and vehicles, required for the project
works would be procured locally with units grouped to promote efficiency and
attract adequate competition; however, bids will also be accepted on
individual schedules to allow small manufacturers to compete. A preference
limited to 15%, or the prevailing customs duty if lower, would be extended to
local manufacturers in the evaluation of bids. The equipment, instruments
and vehicles would be procured locally to benefit from existing servicing,
supply facilities and spare parts availability. These goods would be
assigned throughout the project area so prompt accessibility to spare parts
and established service facilities is essential. There is adequate domestic
competition and prices are reasonable. Standardized procedures and bidding
documents developed by CWC and approved by IDA would also be used to procure
the equipment, materials and supplies.
4.08    Contract Review.  All bidding packages for works estimated to cost
US$0.5 M equivalent or more, and for goods costing US$100,000 equivalent or
more, would be subject to standard IDA pre-action review. These will account
for at least 80% of the procurement value of works and 90% of the goods. The
remaining contracts would be subject to IDA post-action review.
Disbursements
4.09    Disbursements would be made for: (i) 100% of the foreign expenditures
for directly imported equipment; (ii) 100% for locally manufactured goods
procured ex-factory; (iii) 70% for other locally procured goods; (iv) 90% of
expenditures for WALMI; (v) 100% of expenditures for technical services, and
canal communications system; (vi) 65% of expenditures for civil works, (vii)
75% of expenditures on completed ATWs, and (viii) 70% of expenditures on
Residential Buildings for Irrigation Department Staff. Disbursements for
works constructed by departmental forces and for payments of less than Rs.
300,000 for works and Rs. 150,000 for goods may be made against certificates
of expenditures, itemized by sub-project. Documentation for these works
would be retained by GOUP and made available for inspection by IDA during
review missions. Full documentation would be required for all other disbur-
sements. The estimated schedule of expenditure is given in Table T-4. The
semi-annual disbursement schedule and the proposed allocation of the proceeds
of the credit are presented in Table T-5. It is expected that disbursements
would be completed by September 1991. The expected rate of disbursements



-28-
follows the typical disbursement profile in the Indian irrigation sector for
the first three years of project implementation; the rate of disbursement
then remains constant since the project is the first time slice of a long
term development program.
Accounts and Audits
4.10    The project would be subject to uormal Government control and audit-
ing procedures. A resident Audit Officer, representing the State Accountant
General (AG) would audit the project accounts. Although continuous and
annual audits are carried out in a timely manner, the finalization is a
lengthy process during which the Statutory Audit Report is presented to the
State Legislature. It would be essential therefore to have interim state-
ments of account certified as to accuracy and authenticity by an independent
competent auditor. Such statements, certified by the AG or his designate,
supported by an itemized account and a suamAry of the expenditures, would be
submitted to IDA as soon as possible after the end of each fiscal year.
Agreements have been reached with GOUP that it would cause the ID and UPSEB
to: (i) maintain separate accounts on project expenditures kept in accord-
ance with sound accounting principles and procedures; (ii) have these
accounts certified as to their accuracy and authenticity annually by inde-
pendent auditors acceptable to IDA and make the resulting interim statements
of account available to IDA as soon as possible, but not later than nine
months after the end of each fiscal year; (iii) have the project accounts
audited annually in accordance with sound auditing principles and submit the
audit report to IDA as soon as available; (iv) maintain separate accounts for
the O&M expenditures in such a manner that the O&M costs of works constructed
under the project can be identified; and (v) make available complete accounts
and financial statements to IDA for inspection during project review.
V. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
General
5.01    Uttar Pradesh has a well structured administrative organization.
Under the project, GOUP would (i) strengthen the agencies responsible for
execution and management of the project works; (ii) establish procedures to
monitor progress of project implementation and realization of project
benefits; and (iii) establish units to undertake the planning and design of
future stages of the UGC rehabilitation and modernization program.
5.02    Overall responsibility for project implementation would rest with the
Secretary for Irrigation and Energy (IS). Two agencies under his jurisdic-
tion would implement the project. The Irrigation Department (ID) constructs,
operates and maintains surface and public groundwater irrigation schemes and
drainage works in the State; the UP State Electricity Board (UPSEB) is
responsible for construction, operation and maintenance of power transmission
and transformation under the project and also for the procurement and instal-
lation of the mechanical and electrical equipment for hydel stations. In
addition to the construction agencies, the Department of Agriculture (DA)
would be responsible for the agricultural development activities to be
provided once project works are commissioned (para 3.28).



-29-
5.03    A Chief Engineer (CE Level I) has been appointed to act as the
Project Coordinator (PC), reporting directly to the IS through the
Engineer-in-Chief (Chart C-3). He will be responsible for the day-to-day
coordination at the working level between the participating agencies. The PC
will be assisted by a Project Coordinating Unit (PCU) which has been set up
exclusively for the project.  The PCU vill draw up annual implementation and
development programs; requests for the necessary financial provisions for the
program; collate expenditures for reimbursement from IDA; prepare project
reports and summaries of monitoring and evaluation studies; and formulate and
maintain an information link to project beneficiaries concerning all aspects
of the project including the proposed implementation schedule. An agreement
has been reached with GOUP that it would maintain the Project Coordinating
Unit within the ID with a suitably qualified and experienced Chief Engineer
(Level 1) appointed to head the unit.
Irrization Department (ID)
5.04    Existing Organization.  The present organization of ID is flexible
and is adjusted to the work program and progress of on-going projects. An
Engineer-in-Chief heads the ID and is assisted by 35 Chief Engineers (CEs) of
Level-I and Level-II grades. Each CE (Level-II) controls about five circles,
each headed by a Superintending Engineer (SE) who is responsible for three to
five divisions, each of which is headed by an Executive Engineer (EE). A
division, in turn, consists of three to five sub-divisions which are under
the control of Assistant Engineers (AEs).
5.05    Major irrigation structures are designed in the offices of CEs
(Level-II, Design) located in Lucknow and Roorkee. Design Units in the
offices of CEs (Design) prepare general layouts of schemes and undertake
detail structural designs which are then supplied to construction circles and
divisional offices. Although detailed designs of major irrigation works are
carried out in accordance with sound engineering practices, there is con-
siderable room for improvement in the general planning and design of minor
irrigation systems.
5.06    Proposed Project Orxanization.  The ID would be strengthened to
implement the project and introduce improvements in planning, design, con-
struction and O&M. The organization strength at the start (1984185) and end
of the project period (l99019l) is given on Chart C-3. Under the project,
the following additional units would be established:
(a) A Project Co-ordinating Unit and a Project Accounting Unit (PAU)
under the Project Coordinator. In addition to co-ordinating
project activities (para 5.03), the PCU would organize the study
tours supported under the project (para 5.14). The PC would
also be responsible for the Water and Land Management Institute
(WALMI) component.
(b) An Investigation and Planning (IMP) Wing, headed by CE
(Level-II) consisting of: three I&P circles; an Irrigated
Agriculture (IA) Unit headed by a Joint Director Agriculture



-30-
(para 5.07); a Project Monitoring and Evaluation (PME) Unit
which would also be responsible for in-service training require-
ments; and a Quality Control Circle responsible for all aspects
of quality control during construction and commissioning phases
of the project program. The I&P Wing would be responsible for
all detailed investigation and planning required for the
project, for planning of the works program after 1990/91 and for
monitoring and evaluation activities required under the project.
The CE (I&P) would be responsible for the Advisory Planning
Group (para 3.27).
tc) A Design Wing, headed by a CE (level-II), consisting of three
design circles. The wing would be responsible for all detailed
design of channels and structures under the project.
(d) A new Construction Wing headed by a CE (Level-I1), consisting of
four construction circles.
(e) The existing O&M Wing for the UGC system with three OEM Circles
and headed by the CE, Ganga (level-II) and expanded to include
two new construction circles.
Cf) A new Construction and ONM Wing headed by a CE (Level-II),
consisting of three construction circles and two O0M circles.
The new Construction Wing (item d above) would be primarily responsible for
the major works on the main canal (mile 3-22). The Construction/O&M Wings
(items e and f above) would operate mainly below mile 118 on the main canal
with the project command area divided to reflect the organizational strength
of each wing. The organization build-up of the ID through the project period
is given in Table T-7.
5.07    Proiect Staffing Requirements.  The staffing build-up within ID under
the project is shown in Table T-8. Based on the implementation schedule and

-31-
reached with GOUP that it would sanction and fill staff positions in accord-

5.08    Operation and Maintenance.  Each distributary will be operated as a
unit and allowance made for local rainfall variations on irrigation demand.
At peak demand periods, all canals would run at full design discharge; at
other times, main and branch canals would be operated on a varying discharge
basis with distributary systems rotated to reflect the local area demand.
In the kharif season, supplementary irrigation would be provided to make up
water shortages. Generally water supply in kbarif would exceed demand except
for the beginning and end of the season. For the rabi crop, water demand
will always exceed supply and farmers would be notified of their water
allocation early enough to plan their cropping pattern, accordingly.
5.09    Below the watercourse outlet, the entire flow would be supplied to
each turnout on a time rotation basis or 'warabundi' with period of flow
proportional to the cultivable command area of the turnout. The supply at
the turnout would be allocated by turns to each farmer, with the time of a
turn proportional to the area of the farmers' holding. The turn would define
the water rights of individual farmers with respect to quantity and time of
their turn in the rotational cycle. Distribution below the watercourse
channel would follow the present practice of using, where necessary, collec-
tive field channels located along property boundaries except for those areas
where, by agreement of the farmers served, irrigation of kharif rice is
undertaken on a field-to-field basis.
5.10    At present, there is no canal system operating in UP designed to the
standards of the project. Consequently, GOUP has not finalized requirements
of staffing, equipment and facilities to ensure efficient operation and
preventive maintenance of the overall UGC system while it is being modern-
ized. However. the project does provide for equipment and facilities for
known needs during the time slice; it also provides for a comprehensive
management study once sufficient works are completed for project O&M require-
ments to be evaluated. Agreements have been reached with COUP that it would:
(i) carry out, under terms of reference satisfactory to IDA, a detailed
management study for reviewing existing rules, procedures and requirements
for the general management of the UGC system, including the routine operation
and preventive maintenance of the project; (ii) submit by September 30, 1985,
a report summarizing the results and recommendations of the study to IDA for
its review and comment; and (iii) thereafter, taking into consideration IDA's
comments, if any, adopt and introduce such recommendations. In addition,
areements have been reached with GOUP that it would (i) operate and maintain
the UGC system such that the quality of irrigation service proposed under the
project can be achieved; and (ii) on June 1 of each year, commencing June 1,
1985, inform IDA of the annual financial and other allocations made for the
operation and maintenance for both the surface and groundwater irrigation
works under the project ana the overall UGC scheme.

5.11    Proaress Review and ReportinB  Recuirements.  Progress reports would

report would contain brief descriptions of the main events of the reporting

5.12    Monitoring and Evaluation (Annex 16, SDV).  A strong monitoring and
evaluation (K&E) system would be essential to ensure a reliable assessment of

project implementation, and for assessment of project performance. Data
generated through M&E activities would be essential for the preparation of
future development phases of the UCC system. ME activities vould be coor-
dinated by the ID as the main implementing agency.
5.13    The emphasis of MIE activities with respect to irrigation vould be as
follows: (i) technical aspects including project implementation, technical

native canal lining tecbniques including lining of watercourses, channel
design standards including freeboard, and labor intensive construction
methods; (ii) system water management from the headworks to the watercourse;
and (iii) routine monitoring of 06H of the irrigation system. Within the IAP
Wing (para 5.06), the PME Unit vould be responsible for irrigation related
MIE vhilst the IA Unit would cover agricultural MIE activities.  Assistance
would be obtained from Universities or other agencies especially for data
analysis and base-line surveys. As required, the ID would call on the
Department of Agriculture (DA) to assist in M6E of the agricultural perfor-
mance of the project (para 5.17).
5.14    TraininR.  Training of project staff would be organized by the PME
Unit (para 5.06) and would be required for: (i) planning and design, includ-
ing the layout of minor distribution systems; (ii) water allocation;
(iii) land acquisition procedures and legal parameters affecti'j the project;
and (iv) the OM of the modernized irrigation systems. The training would be
"in-service", directed by senior ID officers and other experts and generally
undertaken at the WALMI (para 3.24). The courses would include lectures,
discussions, case studies and field trips. In particular, the courses would
cover planning, design and o0M of improved irrigation systems, eater manage-
ment at the field level, cultivator organization and basic cultivation prac-
tices for principle crops to be grovw in the project comand ax:as. Junior
staff would be trained for about three veeks and senior staff for about one
week. In addition, the PHE Unit would organize the study tours provided
under the project for ID staff (para 3.25).

Uttar Pradesh State Electricity Board (UPSEB)
5.15    General.  The UPSEB is responsible for the construction and O&M of
rural electrification (RE) works in the State and has set up a separate
Public Tubewell Electrification (PTE) Unit for energizing public tubewells.
With respect to proiect tubewells, PTE unit would undertake all planning and
design activities associated with energizing the wells. It would prepare
or approve cost estimates received from field staff and arrange for the
necessary sanctions to implement the works. The unit would also coordinate
procurement and supply of materials to field units, monitor the progress of
works and be responsible for the prompt receipt of work accounts from the
field units. The UPSEB units would coordinate closely with field officers of
the ID. The planning, design and implementation of electrical and mechanical
works for the hydel stations would be undertaken by the units in the UPSEB
responsible for swall hydel development. Close liaison would be maintained
with the design units in the ID responsible for the hydel civil works.
5.16   Oeration and Maintenance (Power Supply).  The O&M of power supply to
each ATW group would be the responsibility of the UPSEB officer in charge of
sub-station from which the dedicated feeder line is taken. He would also
keep daily records of energy sent out to the ATWs from metering facilities at
the sub-station. The AE (O&M) for the area where the ATWs are located would
be responsible for maintenance of 11 kV main and spur lines and transformer
centers supplying individual project tubewells. The O&M staff responsible
for existing hydel stations on the UG main canal would take over the new
stations once they are commissioned.
Department of Agriculture
5.17    The Department of Agriculture's (DA) role in the execution of the
project would be within its broader organizational framework. While the ID
is organized according to canal commands, the DA is organized according to
the administrative units of the State (i.e. divisions, districts, tehsils,
and blocks). Since it would not be practical to alter the organizational
structure of the DA, the project has reached understandings with GOUP that
agricultural extension and research serving the project area would be
strengthened 'para 3.28). However, to ensure that irrigation services meet
the agricultural requirements of the Project, DA staff at State and divi-
sional levels would be members of the appropriate project coordination and
executive committees.
VI. AGRICULTURAL PRODUCTION, MARKET PROSPECT AND PRICES
Cropping Patterns
6.01    The present and expected future cropping patterns in the three dis-
tributary commands to be modernized are shown in Table T-6. The present
cropping patterns are based on data collected by either the lD or Revenue
Department. Actual cropping intensities may be higher than indicated, since
farmers do much unreported water spreading (para 2.17). Waterspreading is
likely to continue after proiect implementation since water supplies would be



-34-
insufficient for farmers to irrigate fully their land holdings in any crop-
ping season.
6.02    The 'future without' project scenario assumes that additional water
supplies from Madhya Ganga canal in kharif will be used for rice. However
the rice area would be less than in the 'future with' project situation
because (i) the unlined canal system would have a lower conveyance efficiency
that the modernized system; and (ii) there would be no ATWs to augment water
supplies. In addition, field percolation losses with rice cultivation are
likely to be higher because applied research to reduce them (para 3.28) may
not be conducted.
6.03    Rabi cropping intensities with the project would increase only
slightly but there would be a change in the crop mix (para 6.04). It is
assumed that all crops would be irrigated nearer to actual water require-
ments, since witb the project, the irrigation service will be predictable,
timely and reliable. Nonetheless, the 'future with' project scenario does
not take into account possible benefits of the applied research program (para
3.28). This may bring about further increases in cropping intensities and
replace some coarse grains (sorghum, pearl millet, barley) with high-value,
high yielding oilseeds and pulse crops, notably rape and grounduuts. These
cropping systems are already practised by a few progressive farmers in the
project area, and extensively in the neighboring Punjab and Haryana States.
Such agricultural changes would not only increase farm incomes but allow
better utilization of irrigation water (Annex 9, SDV).
Crop-Yields and Production
6.C4    Table T-9 shows present and expected yields and production based on
cropping patterns given in Table T-6, and the development scenarios outlined
for the 'future without' and 'future with' project situations. Farmers would
tend to change to higher-yielding varieties of coarse grains and from barley
to wheat. Yields would increase because of improved irrigation service. The
main benefits would accrue in the zaid season when cropping intensities are
expected to more than double.
Input Requirements
6.05    Future requirements of fertilizers and pesticides will depend on
availability rather than marketing and distribution. Since the declared (and
to date successfully pursued) policy of GOI is to bring on stream new fer-
tilizer plants and to increase importation of fertilizer and pesticide
materials not locally manufactured, the project input requirements should be
secure. The additional fodder and crop by-products under the project will
ensure that animal draft power will continue to be adequate for the project's
needs.
6.06    No labour shortages are expected for the distributary command areas
to be modernized, although there could be seasonal scarcities at full project
development. At present, the UGC command relies on temporary labour for
sugarcane and wheat harvesting, although increasing use of threshing machines
is releasing many labourers after the wheat is cut. Labor shortage may occur



-35-
for rice transplanting, harvesting, and threshing. However, water supply for
rice is scheduled for the project only after all other areas relying on the
Ganga river in this season are served. Therefore the labor force transplant-
ing rice should continue to be available when needed in the project area.
Since increase of rice cultivation would be fairly gradual, there will be
sufficient time for the necessary adjustments to take place. Manual thresh-
ing of the rice crop is being superseded by modifications to locally made
wheat threshers available in the project area.
Market Prospects and Prices (Annex 2)
6.07    The project modernizes a large existing irrigated area and thus
market and processing facilities for all main crops are established and can
readily be expanded. Production increases, although substantial in terms of
the immediate areas developed under the project, are small related to the
market. Marketing of surplus produce is not expected to pose any significant
problems since the established trading system through private dealers and
Government markets would absorb the supply. However, many small farmers may
continue to receive unfavorable prices because they sell crops at harvest
time for want of suitable storage and in order to repay crop loans. The
project may therefore generate the need for more storage capacity at local
cooperative levels to allow farmers to store their produce and thus benefit
from higher seasonal prices.
6.08    No major change in crop prices are foreseen because prices are lar-
gely determined by seasonal supply and demand pattern. The Government-s
price supports for sugarcane, rice and wheat have been particularly effective
in maintaining sufficient incentive for farmers, while keeping the real price
to consumers fairly stable. It has therefore been assumed that future
farmgate prices (in constant terms) for paddy and wheat would follow the
official procurement prices of Rs. 1380/ton and Rs. 1520/ton for paddy and
wheat respectively (April 1984). Sugarcane would continue to follow
well-established farugate prices in the areas where sugarcane is the major
crop (about Rs. 220/ton in 1984).
VII. FINANCIAL ANALYSIS
General
7.01    The two main project objectives -  risk aversion and modernization of
the existing UGC system - have important financial implications for both the
State and individual farmers within the UGC command area. In terms of the
first objective, GOUP has realized that delay in replacing works at risk has
endangered the entire UGC system. A failure would be catastrophic since most
of the direct beneficiaries are small farmers who would not survive without a
regular income. To the State, water charges in the UGC command (assessed at
about Rs. 78 N for 1982-83) could not be collected if the UGC system ceased
to operate in any year. Thus, GOUP would face a substantial tax loss. Even
more important would be the loss of other sources of revenue to the State
budget, such as sales taxes. Apart from the financial implications of com-
plete system failure, the poor condition of the existing system has resulted
in serious reduction of its productivity potential in terms of direct losses



-36-
of income and profits to taxpayers and, indirectly, as an important loss of
tax revenue to the State budget. The second project objective, to modernize
the canal distribution system, will substantially improve the utilization of
sunk costs and the project investment which must be made in risk aversion
works. Modernization will significantly improve farmers' income, par-
ticularly in the tail-end sections of canal commands. A consequence of
increased financial benefits to the individual farmers will be an increase of
tax revenue to the State.
Risk to Present Income Levels
7.02    The likely consequences in terms of foregone agricultural production
if part of the UGC system should fail are described in Annex 2. An attempt
has been made to quantify the losses in farm incomes and other consequences
of such a failure for various parts of the UGC command area. Disruption of
canal water supply would result in an average annual loss of farm income of
about Rs. 2,000 per ha for about 400,000 ha (or 33%) of the UGC command.
Another 300,000 ha, presently supplied from the Mat Feeder, vould also suffer
an annual loss of about Rs. 1,200 per ha. Data on present farm incomes and
expenditures are inadequate to assess the impact on different farm sizes,
but, the most seriously affected would be marginal farmers with less than 1.0
ha (62% of all farms) and agricultural laborers.
Farm Incomes from Modernization
7.03    Outlet studies conducted throughout the project area have shown that
present firm incomes vary considerably with location of the farm in the canal
command. There is a significant decrease in irrigation intensity from head
to tail along main, branch and distributary canal systems and also within
individual watercourse command areas. Since the basic project objective in
modernizing the existing system is to provide a timely and reliable irriga-
tion service and increase water supplies to all beneficiaries, assessment of
project impact on farmers' income has been made with farm location rather
than holding size as the dominant variable. The effect on farm incomes has
been examined for a typical farm of 1.0 ha at the head and tail reach of each
distributary commands to be modernized. For both locations on each dis-
tributary, farm budgets have been estimated for the 'present', 'future
without' and 'future with' project situations. The main determinants of
cropping patterns and intensities, household size, and the use of hired labor
have been generated from farm surveys and district statistics. The detailed
assumptions used in formulating model farm budgets are given in Annex 2.
Financial prices, input requirements and individual crop budgets are given in
Tables 1,3, and 4, of Annex 2. Typical farm budgets are given in Table T-10
and incremental farm incomes (after deduction of water and land related
charges) are summarized below:

mainly due to improved timeliness and reliability of the irrigation service;
the tail reach would also benefit from a substantial increase in water sup-

large farms for both crop production and total income.
Impact on Poverty
7.04    Annual incomes per capita 'vith' and 'without' the project are

840 (US$78) j1 per capita then, at present, most farm households live close
to or below this poverty line (Annex 2). At full development, average per
capita income (after deduction of water charges and other taxes) would
increase by between Rs. 450 (US$42) and Rs. 670 (US$62) per annum. The
farmers located at the tail of the distributary systems would experience a
comparably higher income increase than those at the head. Incomes of the
average family on farming units of 1.0 ha or more would exceed the poverty

-38-
7.05    A primary concern will be to assist farmers to obtain the other
inputs necessary to generate the incremental crop production possible with
reliable and equitable irrigation. Nonetheless, there will be small farmers
who may be unable to generate a reliable income from crop production to
sustain the family and provide sufficient cash flow to buy inputs for modern
irrigated agriculture. Therefore, in terms of formulating a cost recovery
policy and appropriate water charges, attention must be given to the weaker
elements of the project population which will remain close to or below the
poverty line, even with full development benefits. To achieve this objec-
tive, a socio-economic study of project beneficiaries would be undertaken
once sufficient project works have been completed and operated long enough to
provide a significant data base. The study would Ci) evaluate the extent to
which the weaker elements of the project population are able fully to utilize
the irrigation service and to purchase other inputs necessary to achieve full
development benefits; and (ii) formulate proposals to enable full development
benefits to the project to be achieved in a timely manner. In this respect,
agreements have been reached with GOUP that it would: Ci) carry out base
line socio-economic surveys of farmers benefiting from the project with terme
of reference established in consultation with IDA; and (ii) submit, by July
31, 1986, a report summarizing the results and recommendations of the study
to IDA for its review and comments.
Repayment Capacity of Farmers
7.06    For each farm model (para 7.03), the incremental land rent or project
rent has been estimated. This is defined as the incremental income less
allowances for depreciation, use of own resources (family labor and bullock
power), perceived risk and own management services. The project rent there-
fore measures the net incentive of farmers to participate in the project. If
the entire project rent was taxed away, this would leave a farmer indifferent
to participating in the project. In practice, the project rent is calculated
for a 'typical' farming situation and can only be estimated within broad
tolerances. With the assumptions used in the analysis, the project rent does
not exhibit significant variations among the various farm models. The level
of project rent seems to be influenced primarily by cropping intensity which
tends to be higher on the smaller farms. An average farm of 1.0 ha would
realize a rent of about Rs. 2,500. Since most beneficiaries would be small
or marginal farmers, it may not be desirable that a substantial portion of
the project rent is made available for cost recovery.
Cost Recovery
7.07    Water Charges.  In general, GOUP has been vigilant with respect to
cost recovery in the irrigation sector. Current water rates are charged on a
cropped area basis, and vary from Rs. 87 per ha for kharif foodgrains (other
than paddy) to Rs. 237 per ha for sugarcane (Table T-11). At present, the
revenue from water charges is about Rs. 400 per ha of irrigated area in the
northern sugarcane growing areas, and declines to about Rs. 150 per ha in the
southern parts of the UGC command. The revenue assessment in the UGC command
area was Rs. 78 N (1982/83). Although in recent years, most of the assessed
revenue is collected, records indicate that the irrigated area in under-



-39-
recorded mainly because the irrigation service is of poor quality. The
expenditure for O&M (including establishment charges) in 1982/83 for the UGC
scheme was about Rs. 51 M (Table T-l1). Although revenue exceeds O&M costs
by Rs. 27 M or 5IO% (1982/83), the O&M of the UGC scheme tends to be under-
funded since the scheme is under modernization. In addition to water char-
ges, the Irrigation and Flood Protection Act provides for collection of a
betterment levy for improvements of irrigation service. However, due to
legal difficulties in its enforcement, the Act is currently suspended.
7.08    Other Azricultural Taxes.  Indirect taxation provides the main source
of public revenues from agriculture (about 90%) and is steadily growing in
real terms. The State Government receives income from sales and commodity
taxes, provisions of support services and rural infrastructure, and charges
for selling produce in regulated markets. The revenue from indirect taxation
declines in the UGC command progressively from north to south with the
decrease in farm incomes because of the difference between the northern
sugarcane areas and the southern part of the UGC system. The revenue for the
UGC scheme, averages about Rs. 90 per ha of net CCA.
7.09    The Potential for Increased Recovery.  It is anticipated that at full
development, the average net income per irrigated ha of CCA in the modernized
areas will increase from Rs. 2,500 to Rs. 4,600 and average rent per ha of
CCA from about Rs. 250 to Rs. 2,500. Consequently, there will be a con-
siderable potential for increasing irrigation charges within the project
command.  However, because water rates are uniform throughout the State
irrespective of the irrigation service provided, their level relates to the
repayment capability of those farmers realizing the lowest irrigation
benefits - typically the tail end farmers in traditionally designed
projects.
7.10    The project will be the first attempt by the State to rehabilitate
and modernize a major existing scheme. Nonetheless, on completion of the
first time slice, the modernized command area will constitute only about 4.0%
of the canal irrigated area within the UGC scheme, and less than 0.5% of the
canal irrigated area in the State. Since modernization of the UGC system
will take place over an extended period (about 30 years), GOUP will have to
address the problem of cost recovery from areas served by a modern irrigation
facilities within the command of an existing canal system. It is unlikely
that GOUP will be able to realize the increased potential for revenue from
higher water rates in modernized irrigation projects, unless the principle of
"statewide uniform rates" is amended. This could be done by introducing a
charge system consisting of two or more tiers, with higher water rates in
modernized projects and new irrigation projects designed to modern standards.
There are precedents to such a tier system in India and in UP. For example,
public tubewell irrigation within UP has water charges set considerably
higher than those for gravity canal irrigation, recognizing the operational
costs of public tubewells.
7.11    Timinz Considerations.  The optimal timing for increasing water
charges has to be carefully assessed. A premature increase (i.e. before most
of the farmers realize the incremental benefits) could be undesirable
because: (i) it may discourage farmers (especially tail-enders) from using



-40-
water and could thus reduce irrigation benefits from the society viewpoint
and further aggravate income inequities; (ii) it could be offset by lower
recovery percentages and high administrative costs; and (iii) further large
increases in irrigation charges during project implementation might reduce
farmers' cooperation and delay the attainment of full development. On the
other hand, the negative aspects of a "late" increase. It is also likely
that it would not be feasible to introduce a differential charge system
before sizeable areas within the State are designed and constructed to
improved irrigation standards and are commissioned, successfully operated and
irrigated areas are realizing the potential benefits. The completed areas of
modern irrigation systems would have to be large enough to warrant the legal
an. institutional changes required to set up a new water charge system.
7.12    Conclusion.  The appropriate time to initiate changes in the water
charge system would be after the performance of the project has been con-
firmed and evaluated. Until then the level of charges can be expected to
relate to statewide rather than project considerations. As the method of
water charging would have a considerable effect on the efficiency of water
use at farm level, the implications of alternative methods of water charging
should be carefully assessed before changes can be recommended. To achieve
this objective, a study would be required before practical proposals for
improvements to the present water charge system can be made. This would
include studies of: {i) legal aspects of various methods of recovery (e.g.
introduction of a two or more tier water charge); (ii) other factors affect-
ing recovery of irrigation charges; and (iii) estimates of the incremental
net income and rent accruing to farmers in modernized irrigation projects.
The study would (taking into account statewide implications) formulate an
appropriate water charge system for existing irrigation schemes which are to
be rehabilitated and modernized over an extended time period. In this
respect, agreements have been reached with GOUP that it would (i) on the
basis of appropriate studies, furnish to IDA for its review and comments by
December 31, 1985, a proposal for establishing a water charge system for
modernized irrigated areas within the command area of existing surface
irrigation schemes, which would take into account the efficiency and
reliability of the irrigation service as well as the quantity of water
provided; (ii) taking into account IDA's comments on this proposal, furnish
to IDA for its review and comment not later than September 30, 1986, a
timetable for applying a new water charge system; and (iii) taking into
account IDA's comments, implement the new water charge system in accordance
with this timetable within the project area. In addition, agreements have
been reached with GOUP that it would: (i) continue to set water charges for
surface irrigation systems with regular reviews of the cost of providing the
service, to ensure that revenue covers the average operation and maintenance
costs and a reasonable share of investment costs of new and modernized
irrigation schemes in the project area taking into account the repayment
capacity of irrigation beneficiaries; and (ii) cause the UPSEB to charge the
ID for electricity for augmentation tubewells constructed under the project
on a metered basis.



-41-
Cost and Rent Recovery under the Proiect
7.13    The assumptions made for estimating rent and cost recovery are given
in Annex 2. Under these assumptions, the rent and cost recovery indices
would be about 13% and 41% respectively. Full recovery of the resources
invested in the risk aversion works over a period of 50 years at 12% interest
per annum would require an average annual recovery of about Rs. 70 per ha of
CCA. The cost of modernization of Bulandshahr distributary would require
Rs. 370 per ha of CCA for full repayment. However, given the reliance of
GOUP on indirect taxation, the actual level of rent and cost recovery cannot
be adequately assessed since increased agricultural income would generate
growth in other sectors of the economy and thus additional tax revenue.
VIII. BENEFITS AND JUSTIFICATION
Project Impact
8.01    The modernization program of the UGC system is likely to take at
least 30 years to complete, depending on finance available and relative
priorities of other development work in the State. The extended development
period means that substantial investments will be made in common works (such
as main and branch canals) in advance of investments in modernizing canal
distribution systems. However, unlike most major projects to be
rehabilitated, the UGC system has deteriorated to such an extent that exist-
ing irrigation benefits are at risk. Thus major investments have to be made
in common works irrespective of whether the UGC system is modernized. Since
GOUP has planned to rehabilitate the UGC system, the risk aversion works have
been designed (at additional cost) to contribute to the modernization objec-
tives.
8.02    The project impact has therefore been evaluated in terms of three
distinct, but interrelated benefits: (i) risk aversion to protect the exist-
ing irrigation system; (ii) modernizing the main canal; and (iii) modernizing
canal distribution blocks. The project impact can be summarized as follows:
(a) Risk Aversion. The project will reduce the risk of failure in
the main canal head reach (para 3.10). Such failure would have
a catastrophic effect on the UGC command area above the point
where the Madbya Ganga canal joins the system (about 720,000
ha). The project also gives priority to main canal works below
the intersection point of the UG and MG canals so that the
irrigation service to about 300,000 ha commanded by the UG main
canal below mile 118 can be guaranteed (para 3.11).
(b) Impact of Common Works. The risk aversion works and other
common works, such as modernizing the main canal, will have a
long-term direct benefit to the overall UGC scheme (1.2 M ha).
The lined reglacement canal under the project will (i) save
about 240 Mm of seepage losses each year; (ii) remove channel
capacity constraints to the lower parts of the UGC command; and
(iii) improve the managability of the UGC system to benefit all
areas served.



-42-
(c) Distributary Modernization. The rehabilitation and modernization
of the existing works will improve the quantity and quality of
irrigation service. Direct benefits typical of full development
of the UGC system would be obtained in the distributary commands
to be modernized under the project (para 3.14). An additional
direct benefit will be that the sunk investments in the HG canal
system can be more quickly realized once the UGC main canal
(mile 118-148) is completed.
8.03    In addition to direct benefits, the project will demonstrate a prac-
tical way to rehabilitate and modernize existing irrigation schemes which are
at or near the end of their useful life. It will also have an important
social benefit in that, for the first time in the State, there would be a
modern surface irrigation development that can deliver an equitable and
predictable water allocation to each farmer. The improved irrigation service
in the modernized distributary commands will demonstrate the impact that can
be obtained from this level of investment. This will include substantial
reduction of the farmer's risk and the provision of incentives for complemen-
tary investments, such as higher input applications and introduction of
higher value crops that require a dependable water supply. Finally, in
addition to direct agricultural benefits, the project includes various
institution building components which will assist the ID to develop its
capability to plan, design, implement and manage irrigation schemes (paras
3.24-3.27).
8.04    The project impact of modernizing distributary canal commands in
terms of increased agricultural production, employment generated and farm
families benefited is summarized below:

Beneficiaries
8.05    The risk aversion components would directly benefit about 1.7 H farm
families whose main source of income would be at risk without the project and
would indirectly benefit a large number of landless laborers. Beneficiaries
would also include owners and employees of the extensive agricultural serv-
ices that depend on the existing UGC system. Within the distributary blocks
to be fully modernized, the project would directly benefit about 53,000 farm



-43-
families, most of which are small or marginal farmers. A considerable
increase in employment would be generated by project construction works and
by increases in agricultural production. Unskilled labor employed in con-
struction work over the next 30 years would be equivalent to about 90,000
full time jobs. The increase of irrigated area and improvement of the exist-
ing system would result in an increase of direct employment. At full
development of the UGC system, the increased demand for project labor for
agricultural production would amount to about 25 M man/days per annum (equiv-
alent to about 110,000 full time jobs). It is estimated that about two thirds
of the demand would be supplied by hired labor, the majority of which would
be landless. The development would also generate additional employment in
local non-farming sectors such as marketing, transport and processing. Thus,
the project would substantially decrease unemployment and the high level of
underemployment in the UGC command area.
Project Analysis
8.06    Only agricultural production benefits have been considered in the
economic analysis. Economic prices for tradeable inputs and outputs are
based on IBRD price projections expressed in 1984 prices. The financial
prices of non-traded inputs and outputs have been adjusted by appropriate
conversion factors, to bring them to the same price basis as the traded ones.
The shadow price of agricultural labor was assumed at 67Z of the present
average wage rates for nired labor. Detailed assumptions on economic
analysis are given in Annex 2.
Justification for Risk Aversion Wnirks
8.07    A technical discussion of the risk aversion works is presented in
Annex 15, SDV; the assumptions used in the justification for risk aversion
works are given in Annex 2. Site evidence indicates that structures on the
UG main canal are liable to failure in the near future but factors which
could cause failure cannot be quantified in terms of a probability indicator.
Under the circumstances, justification for the risk aversion works has been
evaluated by comparing the economic costs of the works with the foregone
benefits which would result if there was a failure. The main assumptions
are as follows:
(a) Costs of Risk Aversion Works. The risk aversion works serve a
multi-purpose function (para 8.02). Under the project, the capacity
of the head reach of the UG main canal would be increased from about
294 to 350 cumecs for operational considerations which relate to
modernization of the entire UGC system. The incremental cost of
providing this extra capacity has been excluded from the risk aver-
sion component, and incorporated as part of the modernization costs
on a pro-rata basis related to canal capacity. Thus, for evaluating
impact, about 842 of investment costs of the main canal works have
been apportioned to risk aversion, with 16Z charged to rehabilitation
and modernization of the overall UGC scheme. On this assumption, the
economic costs of risk aversion works, adjusted by the CCF, are
estimated at Rs. 941 M (US$87.1 M) in April 1984 prices, including
physical contingencies but excluding price contingencies and the cost



-44-
of land acquisition (Table 2, Annex 2). The present value of the
risk aversion costs would be about Re 443 M based on the project
implementation schedule for these works.
(b) The effects of a failure. The economic benefits to risk aversion
works would result from preventing return of a substantial part of
UGC command area (about 400,000 ha) to temporary rainfed conditions
supplemented by whatever groundwater resources that can be utilized
until the damaged works could be repaired or replaced. The main
production loss would be to sugarcane (about 20% reduction in area
and a similar reduction in yield) and the consequential effect on
the sugar processing industries. The net value of production, if
irrigation is discontinued, would decrease (i) by a minimum of about
Rs. 480 H per annum in financial prices or Rs. 510 M (US$47 M) in
economic prices in the year of a major failure; and (ii) in sub-
sequent years until replacement works are completed, by about Rs 880
H per annum in financial prices or Rs 850 M (US$79 H) in economic
prices.
In addition to the loss of direct benefits, a failure, even if temporary,
would have a devastating effect on farm incomes and employment and on
indirect benefits generated from agriculture. Farm employment would decrease
by about 7.1 M man/days per annum (equivalent to about 29,700 full-time
jobs). The demand for hired labor in agriculture and other activities would
decrease and there would be a major contraction in all sectors of the local
economy, depressing wages and profits over much of western UP.
8.08    Timing of Risk Aversion Works.  Although deterioration of the struc-
tures at risk is obvious, the rate of deterioration, probability and type of
failure at any point in time cannot be quantified. To evaluate the conse-
quences of undertaking the risk aversion program too early, an analysis has
been made to assess how long the existing structures would have to last
without failure so that the forgone direct agricultural benefits would equal
the replacement costs (both estimated to net present value discounted at
12%). This evaluation confirms that an immediate replacement program is
justified if failure were to occur within the next 20 years. The technical
judgement is that the structures are unlikely to last that long.
8.09    The above analysis relates to the existing UGC scheme and does not
take account of modernization. The modernization program will involve major
investments and result in farmers adopting agricultural objectives which
require much greater investments in inputs in response to a reliable and
timely irrigation service. If the UGC system is to be modernized, a prereq-
uisite is the removal of the present risk of failure in the main canal. It
has tberefore been concluded that risk of failure is unacceptable since loss
of production and income due to failure would be catastrophic. A failure
would inevitably result in considerable social unrest, particularly as the
extent of the risk is common local knowledge. Although the replacement of
the risk aversion works is planned to take about 12 years, this program could
be accelerated if any of the works at risk shoved an increasing rate of
deterioration.



-45-
Canal Modernization Components
8.10    The rehabilitation and modernization program for the UGC system
consists of two parts: ti) main canal modernization which have an impact
throughout the UGC command; and (ii) distributary blocks where benefits are
confined within the distributary command area. Under the project, works
under item (i) above include sections of main canal required to alleviate
risks to the existing UGC system (mile 3-22 and 118-148) and initial works
below mile 148. Thus, except for the small investment below mile 148, the
main canal works must be undertaken whether or not the overall UGC scheme is
modernized. Thus, apart from risk aversion, the main canal investment will
improve the qualit- and quantity of irrigation service throughout the UGC
system as measured at the head regulators of the canal distribution system.
The improvements to the irrigation service would result from lining and
improving the operational efficiency of the canal.
Main Canal Modernization
8.11   Analysis Assumptions.  The economic costs of the main canal have been
apportioned 84% to risk aversion and 16% to modernization to reflect the
multi-purpose benefits of such works (para 8.07). The economic costs of all
civil works, the canal communication system, land acquisition, administration
and engineering, operation and maintenance have been included in the deriva-
tion of the ERR. The benefits attributable to main canal modernization would
result from saving of seepage losses and improvement to operational
efficiency. At present, the unproductive use of water supplied to the UG
main canal averages about 28Z of the annual head diversion, with seepage
accounting for about 11% of the loss (Annex 6, SDV).  With lining and
improved regulation, the main canal would operate at about 90% efficiency,
compared to a present efficiency of about 72Z. Witbin the UGC command area
which will benefit from main canal modernization, irrigation demand will
always exceed supply throughout the year, including the critical period of
rice planting during the kharif season (para 3.05). It can therefore be
assumed that saved water supplies in the main canal vould translate into an
equivalent increase in irrigated area; the present crop mix and yields would
remain substantially unchanged since the increased water supplies would be
relatively small.
8.12    Economic Rate of Return.  Discounting the apportioned cost and
benefit streams over a 50 year period, canal modernization gives an estimated
ERR of 19% and a net present value at 12% discount rate of Rps 370/ha over
the area commanded by the UGC scheme. These results indicate a clear
economic viability for modernizing the main canal sections included under the
project. The cost and benefit streams are given in Table T-13. Benefits
attributable to the completion of the MGC system (para 8.02), which would
augment the present kharif water supply downstream mile 118 of the UGC main
canal, have been excluded from the economie analysis.



Canal Distributary Blocks
8.13    Analysis Assumptions.  Within canal distributary blocks, water saved
through lining and improved regulation and from ATWs would be used vithin
the distributary command area. Extra water supplies available from coumon
works would be distributed throughout the UCC command to achieve the alloca-
tion pattern described in para 3.06. Since it can be assumed that the two
distributary blocks to be completely modernized under the project (about
39,000 ha) will be typical of the overall development of the UGC system, the
analysis has been undertaken tor these distributary commands. The economic
costs of all civil works, equipment, land acquisition, administration and
engineering, and OEM have been included in the derivation of the ERR.
Incremental benefits from distributary modernization assume that: (i) the
'future without' project situation includes the impact attributed to addi-
tional water supplies from common vorks (para 8.10); and (ii) each dis-
tributary block would be modernized and operated with an apportionated head
discharge equivalent to that available when the UGC system is fully developed
(para 3.06). Thus, an analysis of canal distributary modernization can be
undertaken independent of when thc works are completed in the overall UGC
development program. Benefit build-up relates to the assumed phasing of
modernized areas coming under irrigation, a three-year transition from
present to future cropping intensities, and a more gradual build-up of yields
and input use including adoption of high yielding crop varieties. The cost
of pumping for the ATWs is based on the estimated marginal cost of elec-
tricity for Uttar Pradesh.
8.14    Economic Rate of Return.  Discounting the cost and benefit streams
over a 50 year period, the estimated ERR is 22X and the present value at 122
discount rate is Rps 3,700/ha. The results indi.ate clear economic viability
for the modernization of a typical unit of the eistribution system under the
project. The cost and benefit streams for the analysis are given in Table
T-13.
8.15    Economics of Canal Lining.  Lining of the distribution system down to
and including watercourse channels is an essential project component since,
apart from groundwater development, lining is the only way to increase water
supplies. The decision to line canals was made after examination of several
planning and design alternatives during project preparation. These alterna-
tives included whether all or part of a typical distributary system should be
lined and the types of lining which should be used. In estimating the ERR of
canal modernization, incremental benefits are largely due to those generated
by the reduction of seepage losses. However, there will be substantial other
benefits from lining which cannot be properly quantified. These include: (i)
the greater flexibility in operating a lined canal system, particularly to
serve small and varying crop areas, and to provide water for early kharif
rice nurseries; (ii) the greater reliability of water supply; (iii) the
possibility to spread irrigation benefits equitably and over a large area;
and (iv) the relative cost advantage of reduction in channel cross section
when a canal is lined. Another important benefit from lining saller chan-
nels is that it enables management to be more effective, in that individual
farmers cannot easily tamper with the system. This would help overcome a
major problem with the existing system, namely that local discipline has
virtually broken down due to the unreliability of the irrigation service.
Detailed analysis of the present conveyance and operational efficiency of



-47-
the UGC system is given in Annex 6, SDV. Since at present there are no lined
distributary systems designed to project standards in western UP, the likely
impact of lining has been estimated from data collected from Bank-supported
programs elsewhere in India, where similar canal systems have been modern-
ized.  An important M&E objective of the project will be to confirm the
benefits attributed to canal lining of the various elements of a distributary
system.
Sensitivity Analysis
8.16    A sensitivity analysis was conducted to determine the effects of
deviation from the main assumptions made for determining the economic
viability of the project. The measure of sensitivity used, the 'switching
value', is the value of the variable tested which would reduce the present
value of the project's net incremental benefits discounted at 12% (the
estimated opportunity cost of capital) to zero. The results are summarized
in the following table, with additional tests presented in Annex 2.

Proiect Risk
8.17    The results of the sensitivity analysis indicate that only large
deviations from the basic assumptions would make the project economically not
viable. However, the net benefits and/or the equity of their distribution
could be affected by the following specific events:
(a) Surface Water Supply. GOUP could reduce supplies to the UGC system
by diverting Ganga water to other new and existing projects in west-
ern UP. This risk is small, since the UGC system has a long estab-
lished users' right, and influential farmers would react strongly if
there was a reduction in their existing irrigation supplies. In
addition, GOUP has given an undertaking that it would maintain the
present surface water allocations to the UGC system (para 3.06).
(b) ManaReability of the UGC Scheme. The deterioration of the existing
system is such that many farmers have ceased to respect the need of
discipline for the common good. Present works, which are easy to
tamper with, would be replaced under modernization of the distribu-
tion system. Lining of the minor canal system would make it possible
to identify the miscreants and, more important, make the system
operation predictable and thus manageable.
(c) System Failure. A basic project objective is to avert risks to parts
of the existing UGC scheme but failure could occur before replacement
works are commissioned. Such a failure would be an unavoidable risk,
since the project is planned to alleviate identified risks as quickly
as possible. Nonetheless, the on-going detailed preparation of risk
aversion works would minimize the foregone benefits to the existing
irrigated areas in the case of a failure.
(d) Institutional Capabilitv. GOUP may have difficulty in recruiting
adequate staff qualified for macro-planning and design, monitoring
and evaluation, and the management principles (O&M) of completed
works. These difficulties would be considerably reduced when GOUP
retains an Advisory Planning Group (para 3.27) and provides housing
for such departmental staff (para 3.25).
8.18    Agricultural Objectives.  A possible risk is that the applied
research program either fails to develop techniques for rice that match the
crop water requirements and water availability on which the project cropping
patterns are based or that these techniques are so difficult or time consum-
ing that farmers would be reluctant to adopt them. Apart from equity and
income distribution problems, this may cause some upstream farmers to attempt
to take more than their share of water to maintain their rice area. There-
fore otber farmers would have to replace rice with lower value crops. This
risk ji minor and production increases due to the project have been conserva-
tively estimated. It is therefore concluded that the risk of not attaining



-49-
the assumed crop yield levels and the design cropping patterns (or something
similar in terms of farm income) is small. Moreover, the principal tasks of
the applied research program would be to develop land preparation techniques
to reduce percolation losses in rice paddies and to identify animal-drawn
tillage implements to prepare land adequately for wheat after rice.
8.19    Although the risk of GOUP not proceeding with project works after
completion of this time slice was considered in the sensitivity analysis, it
is regarded as small.  GOUP is fully committed to modernization of the UGC
command area and, with the existing sunk cost and those under the project,
the economic viability of the remaining works would be high. It is therefore
expected that if future financial constraints result in a reduction in the
StateCs overall irrigation development program, GOUP would continue with the
implementation of follow-up phases of this project until its completion.
Thus, the project risks do not exceed those associated with irrigation
projects of similar size and type in India.
Environmental Effects
8.20    The main water-related diseases in UP are malaria and typhoid.  The
construction of drainage facilities and the lining of canals in the project
command would tend to reduce areas of stagnant water which can be breeding
grounds for mosquitoes. However, although the project should have only
beneficial environmental effects, an agreement has been reached with GOUP
that it would take all measures as may be considered necessary to minimize
the risk of malaria and other water-related diseases within the project area.
IX. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATIONS
9.01    Agreements have been reached with GOI that it would:
(a) make the proceeds of the credit available to GOUP on GOI's standard
terms and arrangements for development assistance to the States (para
4.03); and
(b) allocate to GOUP radio frequencies for the UGC communication system by
August 31, 1985 (para 3.21).
9.02    Agreements have been reached with GOUP that it would:
(a)  carry out the planning, design, construction, and operation and
maintenance of the project works in accordance with standards and
criteria acceptable to IDA (para 3.03);
(b) through the UPSEB (i) provide independent electric 11 kV feeder lines
from 132 kV sub-stations to connect all augmentation tubevells; (ii)
ensure that these feeders would remain exclusive to ATWs; (iii)
provide ATWs with continuous power supply when required with a volt-
age fluctuation of not greater than plus or minus 6%; and (iv) ener-
gize each project tubewell system according to a schedule prepared by
the ID not later than September 30, 1986 and acceptable to IDA. (para
3.20)

March 31, 1986 (para 3.24);
(d)  by December 31, 1984, retain Consultants to provide general assis-

acceptable to IDA (para 3.27);

able to IDA, for submission to the Indian Council of Agricultural
Research for its review and comments; (ii) commence applied research
investigations not later than June 1, 1985; and (iii) review and
update the program annually commencing on March 1, 1986; (para 3.28);
(g) promptly provide resources and funds to: (i) execute the project in
accordance with the implementation and expenditure schedules for the
project period, and (ii) operate and maintain adequately and effi-
ciently the irrigation systems under the project. (para 4.03);
Ch) cause the ID and UPSEB to: Ci) maintain separate accounts on project
expenditures kept in accordance with sound accounting principles and
procedures; (ii) have these accounts and financial statements cer-
tified as to their accuracy and authenticity annually by independent
auditors acceptable to IDA and make the resulting interim statements
of account available to IDA as soon as possible but not later than
nine months after the end of each fiscal year; (iii) have the project
accounts audited annually in accordance with sound auditing prin-
ciples and submit the audit report to IDA as soon as available; (iv)
maintain separate accounts for the O&M expenditures in such a manner
that the O&M cost of works constructed under the project can be
identified; and (v) make available complete accounts and financial
statements to IDA for inspection during project review (para 4.10);
Ci)  maintain the Project Coordinating Unit within the ID with a suitably
qualified and experienced Chief Engineer (Level I) appointed to head
the unit (para 5.03);
(j)  sanction and fill staff positions in accordance with an organization
plan and a staffing schedule established in consultation with IDA and
train such staff so that the ID can carry out the project program and
operate and maintain the project works (para 5.07);
(k)  (i) carry out, under terms of reference satisfactory to IDA, a
detailed management study for reviewing existing rules, procedures
and requirements for the general management of the UGC system,
including the routine operation and preventive maintenance of the

project; (ii) submit by September 30, 1985, a report summarizing the
results and recommendations of the study to IDA for its review and
comments; and (iii) thereafter, taking into account IDA's comments,
if any, adopt and introduce such recommendations (para 5.10);
(1) (i) operate and maintain the UGC system such that the quality of
irrigation service provided under the project can be achieved; and
(ii) on June 1 of each year, commencing June 1, 1985 inform IDA of

and the overall UGC scheme (para 5.10);

after the end of each reporting period; and (ii) a project completion
report no later than six months after the closing date of the project

with IDA; and (ii) submit, by July 31, 1986, a report summarizing the
results and recommendations of the study to IDA for its review and
comments (para 7.05);
Co) (i) based on appropriate studies and investigation, furnish to IDA
for its review and comment, not later than December 31, 1985, a
proposal for establishing a water charge system for modernized
irrigated areas within the command area of existing irrigation
schemes; such a cost recovery system would take into account the
efficiency and reliability of the irrigation service as well as the
quantity of water provided; and (ii) taking into account IDA's com-
ments on this proposal, furnish to IDA not later than September 30,
1986, for its review and comment a timetable for applying the new
water charge system, and (iii) taking into account ID-"c comments,
implement the new water charge system in accordance with this
timetable within the project area (para 7.12);
(p) Ci) continue to set water charges on irrigation systems with regular
reviews of the cost of providing the service to ensure that revenue
covers the average operation and maintenance costs and a reasonable
share of investment costs of new and modernized irrigation schemes in
the project area taking into account the repayment capacity of
irrigation beneficiaries; and (ii) cause the UPSEB to charge the ID
for electricity for all augmentation tubewells constructed under the
project on a metered basis (para 7.12); and
(q) take all measures as may be considered necessary to minimize the risk
of malaria and other water-related diseases within the project area
(para 8.20).
9.03    With the above agreements, the project would be suitable for an IDA
credit of US$125.0 M on standard IDA terms. The borrower would be the
Government of India.
