INDIA
UPPER GANGA IRRIGATION MODERNIZATION PROJECT
Assumptions for Economic and Financial Analysis
Price and Conversion Factors
1.      General.  The economic prices of major traded agricultural com-
modities (at farm gate) were derived from the IBRD world market prices (1984
projected through 1995) expressed in 1984 currency values with appropriate
adjustments for freight, handling and processing. Non-traded foodgrains and
other non-traded goods and services were brought to a common basis with the
traded goods and services through the use of conversion factors. Three
conversion factors have been used: (i) Standard Conversion Factor (SCF); (ii)
Construction Conversion Factor (CCF); and (iii) Food Conversion Factor (FCF).
Details on the derivation of the conversion factors and their use in
described below. The opportunity cost of capital (OCC) was assumed at 12..
A standard exchange rate of US$1.0 to Rs 10.8 was used to convert prices
expressed in US dollars to local currency values. The assumptions used to
derive the estimates are described below.
2.      Standard Conversion Factor.  For economic analysis, costs and
benefits of the project have been evaluated at world market prices in order
to measure India's net economic benefits from the project. This requires
certain price adjustments, since many items are not traded in the world
markets and the prices of others are affected by tariffs and trade restric-
tions. To make them comparable, a SCF have been applied to the price of
non-traded goods. In the absence of trade restrictions, the SCF can be
estimated by using the following formula:
SCF  -              + M
X + Sx + m+ Tm
where: X - export value (f.o.b.);
M - import value (c.i.f.);
S - subsidies on exports;
T - taxeL an duties on imports
For the period since 1970, this calculation has produced a SCF of 0.86.
However, since this method does not take into consideration quantitative
restrictions on trade, the estimates derived provide only an upper limit for
the SCF. The Indian Planning Comiss ion recommends to use in the evaluation
of industrial projects, conversion factors for foreign exchange and untraded



-74-
ANNEX 2
Page 2
goods that imply a SCF of about 0.75. This estimate is considered to be
nearer to the lover limit of the real SCF. Consequently, a SCF of 0.8 has
been used in the economic analysis.
3.      Construction Conversion Factor.  The construction of project civil
works require the use of a mixture nf equipment, materials, and skilled and
unskilled labor:
(a) Traded Component: This component includes capital-intensive
works which require imported materials. Hence the conversion
factor of 1.00 has been used. It is estimated that about
5% of the construction costs fall under this category.
(b)  Non-Traded Component.  This component includes works that
require skilled labor and locally manufactured materials.
The SCF of 0.80 has been used as the conversion factor for
these works which include about 45% of construction costs.
(c) Unskilled Labor. A ratio of economic to financial wages equal
to that assumed for agricultural labor (67%) has been assumed
for unskilled construction labor (para 6). It is estimated
that the cost of unskilled labor constitutes about 50Z of the
total construction costs.
The weighted average of these conversion factors would be as follows:
Conversion Factor      Weitht
Traded Items                       1.00              0.05
Non-Traded Items                   0.80              0.45
Unskilled Labor                    0.67              0.50
Construction Costs                 0.75              1.00
(weighted average)
Thus, a CCF of 0.75 has been adopted for converting the cost of construction
wc-ks from financial to economic prices.
4.      Economic Prices of Commodities and Foodirain Conversion Factor (FCF).
The economic prices for traded commodities such as rice, wheat, oilseeds and
sugarcane were derived by using border prices in US dollars according to IBRD
commodity projections and converted to local currency values at the standard
exchange rate. The domestic cost components (such as local transport, han-
dling and marketing charges) were adjusted by a SCF of 0.80. The economic
prices for non-traded foodgrain (maize, pulses and barley were assumed to be
non-traded) were derived by applying a FCF jj to the present financial
prices. The recent sharp downward revision of IBRD agricultural commodity
price forecasts bave brought the world market price of wheat and rice to
1/ Estimated as a ratio of wheat and rice production valued at economic
prices to wheat and rice production valued at average domestic market
prices.



-75-
ANNEX 2
Page 3
parity with  Ae domestic market prices in India.  As a result, a FCF of 1.0
has been use1 in the economic analysis.
5.      Prices cf Agricultural Inputs.  Fertilizers have been valued at the
projected world market price.  Pesticides and insecticides have been assumed
to be traded.  In order to make an allowance for taxes and subsidies and for
the cost of transport and marketing, a conversion factor of 0.95 has been
applied to their financial prices. The economic price of seed and miscel-
laneous charges has been assumed to approximate their financial price. The
economic price of farm power (bullock pair with operator) was derived from
going daily rental rates, adjusted by the SCF. A sunmary of economic and
financial prices is presented in Table 1.
6.      Opportunity Cost of Labor.  It is estimated that demand for labor
would not caange appreciably as a result of the project since agricalture
wages in the project area have been amongst the highest in UP. Based on the
available information from the rural areas in the State and experience else-
where in India, the following assumptions have been adopted for estimates of
marginal cost of farm labor:
(a) At the slack employment periods, the marginal cost of labor
would equal the value of additional consumption needed for family
members to undertake field work (Rs. 0.90 per man/day), 1/ plus
the value of foregone activities such as home improvement
estimated at about Rs. 3.20 per man/day.
(b) As the demand for labor intensifies before the peak seasons
(transplanting of rice and harvesting), the marginal cost would rise
from the minimum average market rate of about Rs. 8.0 per man/day to
about Rs. 10.0 per man/day.
gc) At the time of peak labor demand, the marginal cost would be
equal to the maximum market rate of about Rs. 12.0 man/day.
(d)  lihen the employment is equal to or larger than the estimated
supply of farm labor from the area, the la3or would be drawn
from non-farm activities and supplemented by migrants.
At the time of peak labor demand, the marginal cost would be
equal to the market rate for such periods of about Rs. 12.0 man/day.
The SCF and the FCF (for adjustments under item b above) and appropriate
weights were applied to the various marginal wage rates resulting in an
average ecenomic wage of Rs. 6.0. When compared to the financ al wage rates,
this is equivalent to an unskilled labor conversion factor of about 0.67.
1/ Estimated as 75% of the current average daily consumption of foodgrains
(560 grams per day).



-76-
ANNEX 2
Page 4
Present Situation
8.      A review of the present UGC system in terms of efficiency and opera-
tional performance, availability of surface and groundwater resources, and
agricultural production is given in Annexes 6, 7, 8 and 9, SDV. The review
innicates the following:
(a) The Risk Element. The original design capacity of 6,750 cusecs at
the head of UCC system has been increased to about 10,500 cusecs at
present (Annex 5, SDV). This has resulted in excessive stress on the
Solani aqueduct and three other major cross-drainage structures on
the first 22 miles of UG main canal. A failure of any one of the
structures is a real possibility and has been considerably aggravated
by the lack of proper maintenance (Annex 15, SDV).
(b)  The Existing Canal Distribution System.  Apart from the risk of
failure, a basic problem with the existing UGC system is its low
operational and conveyance efficiency. It is estimated that on
average from every cubic meter of water diverted at headworks at
Hardwar only about 0.2 m3 reaches the farmers' fields. As in most
Indian irrigation schemes, the irrigation distribution system below
the watercourse outlet (commanding about 50 ha) is inefficient.
(c) The Consequences for ARriculture. The existing UGC system cannot
respond to the demands of modern agriculture. The potential for
improvement can be seen in the northern part o' the system, which,
due to its favorable location, benefits from extra irrigation sup-
plies. On the other hand, farmers in the southern part of the com-
mand receive the residual irrigation water supplies. Consequently,
farm incomes in the north average about twice that in the south of
the UGC command. Indeed, in some of the southern parts of the com-
mand, waterlogging can be a concern when excess irrigation supplies
are routed there. What is true for the system wide inequities also
applies to individual distributary and watercourse outlet commands.
(d) The Absence of Discipline. Because of the deterioration of the
irrigation system, the ID is unwilling or unable to enforce dis-
cipline in water allocation among farmers. The North India Canal and
Drainage Act (1874), as amended in 1973,is often ignored and the
efficiency of water use suffers still further. The breakdown of
order is particularly apparent in water-short areas.
Risk Aversion Component
9.      The primary objective 3f the risk aversion component is to ensure the
continuity of irrigation in a command area which is already irrigated rather
than to develop new areas. Its main economic benefits would come from
preventing the project area from temporarily reverting to rainfed conditions.
10.     The Effects of Failure.  The technical status of the four cross
drainage structures is described in Annex 15, SDV. Failure would most likely
occur during the second half of the monsoon period due to high floods in one
of the four torrent rivers that cross the main canal between mile 3 and 22.
The economic consequences of a failure are expected as follows:



-77-
ANNEX 2
Page 5
(a) Areas Affected. The interruption of water supply would directly
affect the upper 400,000 ha of the UGC command area which has no
alternative way of receiving a surface water supply. At mile 118,
the UG main canal is joined by Madhya Ganga (MG) main canal; there-
fore once the MGC is operational, there would be an alternative way
of supplying the UGC command from the Ganga, thus protecting about
500,000 ha. In addition, 300,000 ha below mile 118 that are
presently supplied through the Mat Feeder (off-take at mile 110 on
UGC) could also be directly affected; after completion of MGC
(expected 1988), this area would be able to receive canal water from
an alternative supply source on the river Ganga (Map IBRD 17648R).
However, since this would occur well before completion of the risk
aversion works under the project (1996), this area would be affected
equally in the 'future with' and 'future without' project situations.
(b) Production Loss in Kharif. At the time of likely failure, rice and
sugarcane yields would be reduced by about 60Z and 15% respectively;
other kharif crops would yield on average about 10% less than if
irrigation supplies continued. This would result in an average loss
of about Rs 500/ha in the upper 400,000 ha of the UGC command area.
(c) Production Loss in Rabi and Zaid. The area planted to wheat would
decline by about 50% and yields would diminish also by about 50Z.
Low value, drought resistant crops would be substituted for wheat
and other high value crops. The sugarcane area would decline by
about 20% and a fodder shortage is likely to develop. Zaid cropping
would no; be possible except for areas served by tubewells. The
combined loss in the rabi and zaid seasons would amount to abcut
Rs/ha 700 in the upper 400,000 ha of the UGC command.
id) Permanent Loss. If the canal water supply could not be resumed
within a year, kharif crops would be planted later and yields would
further decline. The decrease in the net value of production, if
irrigation is discontinued through a whole annual cropping cycle, is
estimated at about Rs. 850 M in economic prices in the northern
400,000 ha which cannot be served through the MGC system and about
Rs. 380 M in the 300,000 ha bupplied through the Mat Feeder. The
possibiiity of the latter loss would be eliminated by the completion
of the MGC system.
(e) Indirect Consequences. Farmers would try to substitute for the lost
canal water by increased investment in private tubewells and an
increase in pumping of existing private wells. However, investment
in private groundwater development would be restricted by implementa-
tion capacity and availability of investment finance. The demand
for farm labor would decrease. Losses in agriculture would set off a
negative multiplier effect in other sectors of the local economy.
The sugar processing industry and other agriculture related
industries would be particularly affected. However, these consequen-
ces have not been taken into account in the analysis.



-78-
ANNEX 2
Page 6
11.     Costs of Risk Aversion Component.  At present the ID undertakes only
emergency maintenance on the four cross drainage structures, primarily
because the UG main canal is operated almost continuously. While the ID has
planned for the ultimate replacement of all the UGC system, regular main-
tenance is underfunded (particularly with respect to major works). The
economic costs of risk azersion works, adjusted by the CCF, are estimated at
Rs. 941 M in April 1984 prices, including physical contingencies, but exclud-
ing price contingencies and the cost of land acquisition (Table 2). Under
the project, the capacity of the UG main canal will be increased from about
9,000 to 12,500 cusecs for various operational considerations, which relate
to modernization of the overall UGC system. The incremental costs of provid-
ing for this extra capacity have been excluded from the risk aversion com-
ponent and incorporated as part of the costs of modernization on a pro-rata
basis, related to canal capacity. The present value of the replacement costs
on the proposed work schedule would therefore be about Rs. 443 M and far less
than one year loss of agricultural production (Rs. 850 M). The replacement
of the risk aversion works is planned to take about 12 years but this program
could be accelerated if any of the works at risk showed an increasing rate of
deterioration.
12.     Timizng of Replacement Works.  Although deterioration of the struc-
tures is obvious, the rate of deterioration and probability of failure at any
point in time cannot be quantified. However, it has been assessed that risk
of failure is unacceptably high and that losses of production and income due
to failure would be catastrophic. A large number of farmers would be
affected and failure would inevitably result in considerable social unrest,
particularly now that the extent of the risk is common local knowledge. The
comparison of replacement costs and foregone agriculture benefits for the
period required to replace the works at risk have been estimated in terms of
net present value (discounted at 12%). This evaluation confirms that an
immediate commencement of replacement works is justified if it is predicted
that a failure could occur within the next 20 years. The conclusive techni-
cal judgment is that the structures are unlikely to last for that period.
Finally, the modernization of the canal distribution system will involve
major investment-, and result in farmers adopting agricultural objectives
which require much greater investments in inputs and assume a reliable,
timely irrigation service. Clearly if the UGC system is to be modernized, a
prerequisite is the removal of the present risk of failure in the main canal.
Modernization of Main Canal
13.     Present Conveyance Efficiency.  The characteristics and performance
of the existing UG main canal are analysed in Annex 6, SDV. The wettVd
surface of the main canal at full supply level (FSL) is about 132 Nft with
wetted perimeters ranging from about 100 ft at the tail to more than 240 ft
near the head. Seepage losses have been estimated on long reaches of the
canal as part of the feasibility study for the project. The seepage loss
rate is about 8.5 cusecs/Mft   aad with the total wetted perimeter at FSL for
the entire main canal of 132 Mft implies a loss rate of 1,122 cusecs.
Expressed as a portion of the diversion capacity at the head of 10,500
cusecs, this loss is about 11X of the diverted water, indicating a conveyance
efficiency of about 90% at FSL.



-79-
ANNEX 2
Page 7
14.     An attempt has been made to estimate total conveyance losses (seepage
and operational) on the main canal by difference, using a gross balance
derived from ID records (data from 1971-1979) of total volume diverted
annually and by seasons to the main canal head, and declining diversions into
branches, distributaries, escapes and deliveries to the LGC system. This
water balance gives an overall conveyance efficiency of about 72%. The high
losses result from (i) the canal flows at less than FSL for most of the year
(particularly in rabi and zaid) which implies a higher loss rate as a propor-
tion of flow diverted, and (ii) unaccounted operational losses.
15.     Modernization Benefits.  The planning, design and management prin-
ciples of the rehabilitated and modernized UG main canal are given in Annex
10, SDV. The modernization of the main canal would result in a significant
improvement in the overall conveyance efficiency. The seepage losses in the
main canal at FSL would be reduced from 11% at present to about 2%. The
constraints in the conveyance capacity at the tail of the canal would be
remove.-. Moreover, new control structures and canal communication system
would reduce the presently high operational losses. The overall conveyance
efficiency of the main canal would increase from the present 72% to about
90%.
16.     The savings of water as a result of higher conveyance efficiency of
the modernized main canal would be used in the lower part of the UGC system
commanding about 600,000 ha (below the sugarcane zone). This will increase
the quantity of water in the tail end of the delivery system and improve the
reliability and predictability of the irrigation service. The impact of the
works has been assumed to be proportionate to the rate of progress in lining
the main canal. Since the demand for irrigation in an average year would
always exceed supply it has been assumed that the increment in the overall
conveyance efficiency would translate into an equivalent increase in
irrigated area with the cropping pattern and yield levels unchanged.
17.     The Economic Rate of Return and Net Present Value.  Discounting the
apportioned capital costs adjusted by the CCF, including physical contingen-
cies and the opportunity cost of land and the benefit streams expressed in
1984 constant prices gives an ERR of 19% and a NPV of Rs. 257.3 M. The cost
and benefit streams are given in Table 7.
Distributary Modernization.
18.     Crop Budgets and Cropping Patterns.  Assessments of crop yields and
cropping patterns, for the 'future with' as well as 'future without' project
situations, were based on (i) farm surveys in the project areas; (ii) yield
statistics; and (iii) direct information from the field. Project yields
represent an estimate of average crop yields which can be expected in an
average year, assuming satisfactory supporting services, (including an effec-
tive agricultural extension service).  At present, the most imortant crops
are wheat, sugarcane and paddy which each account for about 20% of total
revenue generated and the intensities of these crops are not expected to
change significantly with modernization. Care has been taken to exclude from
the economic analysis those benefits which would result from completion of
the MGC system which will augment kharif water supply in the UG main canal
below mile 118.



-80-
ANNEX 2
Page 8
19.     Direct Benefits.  Estimates of crop production, yields, inputs and
farm incomes at 'present', 'future without' and 'future with' project situa-
tions are based on the crop yields and production input data given in Table
3, and on the following assumptions:
(a) Crop Yields and Inputs. A five-year build-up period has been assumed
for transition of yield levels from the 'present' to 'future with'
project situation. The build-up rate was based on historic produc-
tivity trends. It was also assumed that crop yields in the 'future
without' project situation would increase from present yield levels
by about 10% in the next eight years.
(b) Cropping Intensities. Experience from irrigation projects elsewhere
in India suggest that increases in cropping intensity to the full
development level will be faster than increases in crop yields. A
build-up period of three years has therefore been assumed for transi-
tion from 'present' to 'future with' project intensities. The
'future without' project cropping pattern is also expected to change
mainly due to the completion of NGC (Table T-6, Main Report). A
prerequisite to attain the rice area postulated under the project
would be reduction of percolation losses in ricefields. Suitable
cultural practices would be formulated under the adaptive research
program. The success of this program presents a small risk since
equivalent programs on similar soils in other parts of the
Indo-Gangetic Plain have achieved this research objective. The
program would mainly entail identification of the right farm imple-
ments and techniques for their use on the farmers' fields.
gc) Rate of Modernization. The rate of completion of modernized irriga-
tion blocks under the project was based on the current implementation
proposals of the ID.

20.     Crop and Farm Budgets.  Using project yields and production input
data (Table 2), individual crop budgets at both financial and economic prices
(Tables 4 and 5 respectively) have been calculated for the main crops for
the 'present' situation and the 'future without' and 'future with' project
situation at full development. Table 6 gives the average crop budget for the
various seasonal cropping patterns and for a typical cropping calendar.
21.     Modernization Benefit Streams.  Based on the assumptions given in
para 15 above, an incremental benefit stream have been estimated for Har-
duaganj distributary command which is typical of distributaries to be modern-
ized under the project (Table 8).
22.     Costs of Distributary Nodernization.  The capital costs of all civil
works within a typical distributary command including the capital costs of
the augmentation tubevells, charges for engineering and administration and
physical contingencies were based on April 1984 contract rates adjusted by
the CCF. Land acquisition requirements under the project are small except
for the main canal replacement. The land lost to agriculture production as a
result of construction was valued at the net economic return per unit area
under the 'future without' project situation. The net return foregone each
year was treated as a recurrent project cost.
23.     Operation and Maintenance Costs.  In 1982/83, rhe budget allocation
for O&M of the existing UGC system was about Rs. 66 per irrigated ha (or 43
Rs/ha of GCA). This provision included costs of establishment and routine
works but excluded special repairs such as damage or failure of individual
structures which in recent years have averaged about Rs. 20 per irrigated
hectare. Recent studies have indicated that about Rs. 100/ha would be needed
to adequately provide for effective operation and preventive maintenance.
However, for the purpose of the analysis it has been assumed that there would
be no incremental cost over present O&M costs of the existing UGC canal
system as estimated by the ID. .] The average annual operation costs of ATWs
as well as maintenance requirements were based on experience from the
on-going State tubevell program (Annex 12, SDV).
j1/ Under the project, a detailed study will be made of the management
requirements of the UGC scheme including the assessment of realistic O&M
costs so that the system can provide the irrigation service necessary to
achieve the predicted project benefits (Annex 10, SDV).

24.     The Economic Rate of Return and Net Present Value.  Based on the
above assumptions, the ERR and the NPV were estimated for the two modernized
distributaries. Discounting the cost and benefit streams over 50 years at
12X discount rate gives the following results:
Typical Distributary Command Area          ERR            NPV
(Rs/ha)
-   Bulandshahr                     18%          2,470
-   Harduaganj                      25%         4,330
The cost and benefits for the two distributaries to be fully modernized
under the project are given in Table 9.
25.     The Economics of Canal  Lining.  The only benefits quantified and
used in the economic analysis are production increases as a result of expan-
sion of irrigated area and increased crop yields due to reduced seepage
losses from canal lining. Full benefits from canal modernization are assumed
to accrue by the fifth year after completion of works. This is a conserva-
tive assumption since in the normal operation of the canal system, the ID and
farmers served, both operate and plan to accommodate substantial year-to-year
variations in water supplies available in the UGC system. Additional
benefits, such as reduced waterlogging and deep percolation, less water
travel time, improved op rational efficiency, and ease of management have not
been quantified or included in project benefits. Seepage loss reductions
fr m canal lining are conservatively estimated to average about 6.5 cusecs/M
ft of wetted perimeter (Annex 10, SDV).
26.     Economics of Watercourse Modernization.  Benefits include reduction
in seepage losses, improved operation, more reliable water deliveries, and
more equitable distribution of water within each watercourse CCA. However,
benefits are estimated mainly on the basis of increased irrigated area
resulting from reduced seepage losses. Most farmers in the UGC command area
are fully accustomed to irrigation, and watercourse improvements on each chak
will be clearly visible to farmers. The increase in stream size of water
deliveries will be apparent, particularly in watercourse tail areas, as soon
as watercourse modernization works are completed. Benefits from the works
for each chak will therefore start to accrue by the first year following
completion of the watercourse.
27.     Economics of Au2mentation Tubewells.  ATWs under the project are
expected to add about 50 !-4m of water to the UGC system at full development.
The existing UGC canal system can easily accommodate this amount of addi-
tional water and farmers can readily use it. Since there has been no pre-
vious experience of conjunctive surface and groundwater use in the project
area and the additional supply of groundwater from ATWs will be small com-
pared to surface water supplies, ATWs have aot been evaluated separately from
other investments under the project. However, the marginal value of conjunc-
tive groundvater use will be high since the ATWs can be operated at full
capacity to supplement surface supplies when river flows are low or at peak
irrigation demand periods. The ATW program has been considered a pilot
operation, which vill be fully evaluated when sufficient wells are commis-
sioned to provide a statistically significant data base.

Sensitivity Analysis
28.     Several tests were performed to evaluate the sensitivity of project
assumptions made in the economic analysis (tabulated in para 8.13, Main
Report). Additional details relating to these assumptions are as follows:
(a)  Commoditv Prices.  The most important crops grown in the UGC system
at present, and expected to be grown in the future, are wheat, sugar-
cane and paddy. A reduction in the price of wheat by 25% would
reduce the ERR by less than one percentage point. A similar reduc-
tion in the price of sugarcane or paddy would have about the same
effect.
(b) Implementation Schedules. The effects of construction delays would
result in higher overall project costs and would slow down the tran-
sition process to future benefits in the modernized command areas.
Such a delay could result from institutional problems, shortages of
key construction materials or problems with land acquisition. The
largest impact on the ERR would be through delays in the completion
of watercourses. A delav of three years in the completion of water-
course lining would reduce the ERR to 10%. For this reason, modern-
ization of watercourses has been made a separate component under the
project so that it can be carefully monitored.
(c) BudRetary Constraints. If there were serious budgetary constraints
during the project period, restricting investments that GOUP could
make to the project, this would reduce the rate of modernization
works in favor of giving priority to risk aversion works. If the
progress of modernization were reduced to half the rate assumed under
the project, the ERR would decline to about 16X.
Cost and Rent Recovery
29.     Estimates of cost and rent recoveries used in this report (Table T-10
and T-12) were based on the assumption that water related charges would
remain unchanged (in real terms) over the project life time. The increment
for indirect cost recovery per ha from taxes on agricultural production at
full development is expected to reach about Rs. 50 to 120/ha depending on
location and 0&M cost would amount to Rs. 100/ha. Cost and revenue streams
are discounted at 12% per annum over 50 years in constant 1984 prices. Under
these assumptions, the rent recovery indexes relate to the complete modern-
ization of the overall UGC scheme.  The present va'ue of the resources
invested in the project discounted over a period of 50 years at 12% per annum
is equal to about Rs. 4,500 per ha of CCA. The present value of the average
annual recovery through water related charges and agricultural taxes over a
50-year period at 12% annum is estimated at Rs. 1,850 per ha of CCA. The
Cost Recovery Index would therefore amount to about 41% (Table T-12).

INDIA
UPPER GANGA IRRIGATION MODERNIZATION PROJECT
Terms of Reference for Advisory Planning Group
Background
1.      The original project proposals were prepared by the Irrigation
Department (ID) in the early 1970s. They followed the traditional planning
and design concepts of the period in India after independence. In 1979, COUP
retained WAPCOS to produce a Feasibility Report (FR) which was completed in
December 1982. The report incorporated many new ideas and concepts for the
planning, design and management of the proposed irrigation systems which are
novel in UP and now need to be translated into practice. In addition, the
project organization will require a substantial increase in implementation
capacity at all staff levels both for planuing, design and construction if
the proposed implementation programs are to be achieved.
2.      Under the circumstances, GOUP has agreed that an Advisory Planning
Group (APG) would be retained to advise and assist the ID. The terms of
reference would be: (i) translating the concepts of the Feasibility Report
of the Upper Ganga Composite Irrigation Project into detailed planning and
design procedures; (ii) testing alternative design solutions; (iii) estab-
lishing least cost solutions for meeting the performance criteria established
for the irrigation system layouts; and (iv) advice on drainage and improved
operation and maintenance procedures so that both existing and modernized
irrigation systems can be managed efficiently; forulating a program for the
comprehensive and thorough monitoring and evaluation (M&E) activities in the
irrigation sector to complement the ID's efforts with respect to M&E and, in
particular, ensure that sufficient data is collected to effectively evaluate
the work program completed under the project (the first time slice of the
overall modernization of the UGC system).
3.      The advantages of having an APG to assist the ID is that it would
provide a group of experienced multi-disciplinary professionals who are not
burden with routine administrative duties or a work program outside the
project.
Advisory Planning Group
4.      GOUP would retain the services of a Consultant Group experienced in
planning, design and implementation of modern irrigation schemes; the group



-110-
ANNEX 4
Page 2
could be provided by either an international or local firm or a joint ven-
ture. In addition to technical expertise, the Consultants would need to be
familiar with practices and procedures of State Irrigation Departments and
of IDA-assisted projects in the irrigation sector in India. The areas in
which consultants' advice could be sought may include:
General
(a) Provide advice, assistance and in-service training to strengthen the
ID's capacity to plan, design, execute, monitor and evaluate and
manage works of the type proposed under the UG Irrigation Modern-
ization Project;
(b) assist the ID in preparing annual implementation schedules for UGC
scheme and, in particular, advising the ID on works programs to
minimize interference to existing irrigated service to the project
command areas;
(c) assist the ID in the study of conjunctive use of groundwater and
surface irrigation supplies in the project area and in the formula-
tion of proposals for the integrated groundwater development includ-
ing activities in both the public or private sectors; and
(d) in particular, advise GOUP on agricultural and socio-economic aspects
of the project and overall rehabilitation and modernization of the
UGC system.
Macro PlanninR
(a) Assist GOUP in formulating improved designs and criteria and in the
preparation of designs for irrigation layouts down to the 45 ha
outlets, channels and structures (except for major works such as the
main canal);
(b) assist the ID in identifying data and monitoring requirements to
improve the system irrigation efficiency of the project (particularly
with respect to canal lining) and then formulating a least cost
solution for the works to be constructed;
(c) assist the ID in the formulation of technical specifications and
typical tender documents suitable for the procurement procedures
(both ICB and LCB) under the project;
(d) assist the ID in setting up project quality control units; and
(e) assist and advice the ID in the detailed preparation of future time
slices of the first phase development (i.e. up to about year 2000) of
the UCC modernization program and provisional schedule and scope in
modernizing the overall UCC scheme.



-lll-
ANNEX 4
Page 3
Micro Planning
(a) Assist the ID in preparing a program for surveys and investigations
required to ensure that project implementation schedule are met in a
timely manner;
(b) advise the ID on the preparation of detailed guidelines for design
of irrigation systems below the watercourse outlet so that works can
be constructed on a block-by-block basis and with minimum inter-
ference to the existing irrigation service; and
(c) assist the ID in setting up procedures and techniques to evaluate
lining of guls (watercourses) below the outlet structure.
Minor Drainaze
(a) Advise the ID on design and implementation of minor drainage works
under the project; in particular, advise on design criteria, standard
outfall and access structures, and any remedial or additional works
needed to utilize natural main drainage channels; and
(b) assist the ID in the preparation of TORs of the project drainage
study and, then, assist in the supervision of the investigations and
evaluation of the results.
Monitoring and Evaluation
(a) Advise the ID on M&E activities which would be required for prepara-
tion of future development phases of the project and on the implemen-
tation of such activities; in particular, advise on the quality and
quantity of data to be collected so that evaluation can be made as
soon as possible and with acceptable degrees of certainty;
(b) advise and assist the ID with the following M&E tasks with respect to
irrigation works and service:
(i) technical aspects including project implementation; technical
performance; design standards; specifications and least cost
solutions; alternative canal lining techniques including lining
of watercourses from the outlet structure to end of the lined
channel; channel design standards including freeboard; and labor
intensive construction methods;
(ii) irrigation system management from head works to watercourse
outlet; and
(iii)  routine monitoring of operation and maintenance of the existing
and modernized irrigation systems.
(c) advise and assist the ID with the following M&E tasks outside irriga-
tion including: preparation of terms of reference for socio-economic
baseline surveys; agricultural performance of the project; and
requirements and availability of agricultural input, services and
facilities necessary to achieve project benefits.



*-112--
ANNEX 4
Page 4
Water ManaRement
(a) Assist the ID in evaluating the management requirements for irriga-
tion works under the project and the problems associated with transi-
tion from existing to improved management procedures; and
(b) assist the ID in the preparation of terms of reference for a detailed
management study and, as required, the preparation of the study. The
basic objectives of the study would be to assist the ID in estab-
lishing rules, procedures and requirements for the general manage-
ment, together with routine operations and preventive maintenance of
the UGC system. In particular, the study would:
(i) assist GOUP identify organizational changes which will have to be
made to efficiently and effectively manage the works under the
project and the existing UGC scheme;
(ii)  review planning of water utilization and allocation including the
integrated operation required to meet the multi-purpose needs of
the project schemes, namely, industrial and domestic needs and,
in the kharif, rabi and zaid seasons irrigation;
(iii) assist the ID on technical or project implications of existing
water agreements that might be necessary under the North India
Canal and Drainage Act (1873) as amended, to ensure that project
benefits are achieved; in particular, how the Act may be amended
to assist in the equitable allocation of scarce water resources;
(iv)  assist the ID in the preparation of O&M procedures for UGC scheme
to include the operation of irrigation systems diverting various
water supplies from the Ganga above Narora Barrage and allocation
of water resources in Western UP; and
(v) assist the ID in the water charge system study to be under the
project.
Conditions of Employment
5.      The Advisory Planning Group would be responsible to the Project
Co-ordinator through the Chief Engineer (Investigation and Planning) who
would be directly responsible for the project planning (see Chart C-3). The
APG would function only in an advisory role. The tentative plan would be for
the Consultants to establish a project office in New Delhi or Meerut, with
adequate technical staff supported by Senior Consultants from their Read
Office. The location of the office would be decided once the composition of
APG is finalized. The initial agreement between GOUP and the Consultants
would be for three years, subject to satisfactory performance; this would be
extended by mutual agreement, if necessary. The local costs may be supported
from the Project Preparation (PP) Fund which was established under the Second
Uttar Pradesh Public Tubewells Project C::. 1337-IN). The PP Fund has been
instru.mental in gradually strengthening the State's ability to plan and
prepare irrigation and multi-purpose water resources project and also provide



-113-                             ANNEX 4
Page 5
opportunities for the domestic consulting industry. Local coats for techni-
cal assistance would be 100X reimbursable under the project, including any
costs for facilities and service required by the Consultants so that they can
function effectively. If foreign consultants are retained, the forei2n costs
of consultants for APG would either be covered under the project or, through
bilateral aid. Any bi-lateral aid would be subject of a separate agreement
between GOI/GOUP and the Government of the donor country.
6.      The Advisory Planning Unit would consist of two Senior Engineers:
one would be experienced in planning, design and implementation of modern
irrigation systems and the other would have similar experience but with more
focus on the operation and maintenance and general management of such sys-
tems. The APG vould also include the services of a Senior Agriculturalist
and a Senior Project Economist primarily to assist and advise the proposed
Monitoring and Evaluation Unit and the units responsible for future planning
activities. In addition, experts in groundwater, irrigation lav, irrigated
agriculture and hydrology would be provided by the APG on short term basis as
required.
7.      The ID would provide sufficient transportation, equipment and
facilities for the APG to function efficiently and such provisions would
revert to the ID on the termination of the consultancy. The ID would also
provide necessary support staff to assist the APG to fulfill their obliga-
tions. The provisional cost estimates of the APG are given in Table 10,
Annex 3 of the Staff Appraisal Report.
